Disliked Acts in Slaughter and Their Effect on Permissibility
List of disliked acts in slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disliked acts in slaughter according to the Hanafi school and explain why they do not prohibit the meat.

Core Idea
Disliked acts like nakh' and cutting the head cause extra pain but the meat remains halal if the vessels are cut while the animal is alive.

This lesson covers the various actions that are makruh (disliked) during slaughter: severing the spinal cord (nakh'), cutting off the head, slaughtering from the back of the neck, dragging the animal, skinning before the body cools, and facing away from the qibla. The common theme is unnecessary suffering. However, these acts do not render the meat haram, as long as the essential cuts are made while the animal is alive.

Guided Path
1
List the disliked acts mentioned in the passage
Go through the text and identify each action explicitly labeled as makruh: nakh', cutting the head, slaughtering from the back, dragging, skinning before cooling, facing away from qibla, and also the act of sharpening after laying down (from previous lesson).
The text says 'وكره النخع وقطع الرأس والذبح من أن يصل إلى النخاع' and later 'وكره أن يجر ما يريد ذبحه وأن يسلخ قبل أن يبرد' and 'ويكره أن يذبحها موجهة لغير القبلة'.
2
Understand the evidence for the dislike
The text mentions a prohibition hadith: 'عن أن ننح الشاة إذا ذبحت' which is the basis for disliking nakh'. For others, the reason is increased pain (زيادة تعذيب).
The hadith is not quoted directly but referenced.
3
Apply the principle that increased pain does not invalidate
The key statement: 'لأن الكراهة لمعنى زائد وهو زيادة الألم فلا يوجب الحرمة'. So these are makruh tanzihi, not haram or invalidating.
4
Distinguish between valid and invalid slaughter
The last sentence sets the condition: if the animal remains alive until the vessels are cut (while the animal is alive), it is halal; if it dies before, it is carrion.
The vessels are the jugular veins and windpipe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وندب حد شفرته لقوله عليه الصلاة إن الله كتب الإحسان على كل شيء فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة وإذا ذبحتم فأحسنوا الذبحة وليحد أحدكم شفرته وليرح ذبيحته» رواه مسلم وغيره ويكره أن يضجعها ثم يحد الشفرة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لمن أضجع الشاة وهو يحد شفرته لقد أردت أن تميتها موتتين هلا حددتها قبل أن تضجعها الحديث والآلة على ضربين : قاطعة وغير قاطعة والقاطعة على ضربين : حادة وكليلة ؛ فالحادة اختيارية وضرورية، فالحادة يجوز الذبح بها من غير كراهة والكليلة يجوز الذبح بها ويكره لما مر من الإبطاء في الإراقة؛ كذا في المحيط . قال رحمه الله : (وكره النخع وقطع الرأس والذبح من أن يصل إلى النخاع وهو خيط أبيض في جوف عظم القفاء) النخع هو الرقبة وهو بالفتح والضم لغة فيه. قال في النهاية ومن قال هو عرق أبيض فقدسها. واعترضه صاحب العناية أن من سمى بما ذكر لم يغلط لأن أهل اللغة ذكروه بلفظ الخيط وإنما كره لنهيه عليه الصلاة والسلام عن أن ننح الشاة إذا ذبحت وتفسيره ما ذكرنا. وقيل: أن يمد رأسها حتى يظهر ،مذبحها وقيل أن يكسر رقبتها قبل أن تسكن من الاضطراب وكل ذلك مكروه. وفي قطع الرأس زيادة تعذيب فيكره، ويكره أن يجر ما يريد ذبحه وأن يسلخ قبل أن يبرد ويؤكل في جميع ذلك لأن الكراهة لمعنى زائد وهو زيادة الألم فلا يوجب الحرمة. ويكره أن يذبحها موجهة لغير القبلة لمخالفة السنة في توجيهها للقبلة وتؤكل وفي الذبح من الفقا زيادة ألم فيكره، ويحل لما ذكرنا إذا بقيت حية حتى يقطع العروق لتحقق الموت بالذكاة، وإن ماتت قبل قطع العروق لا تؤكل لوجود الموت بما ليس بذكاة.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And it is recommended to sharpen his blade because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence in everything. So when you kill, do so well; when you slaughter, do so well; let one of you sharpen his blade and let him give comfort to his animal.' Narrated by Muslim and others. And it is disliked to lay it down and then sharpen the blade because of his saying, peace be upon him, to one who laid down a sheep while sharpening his blade: 'You intend to give it two deaths; why did you not sharpen it before laying it down?' The tool is of two types: cutting and non-cutting. The cutting is of two types: sharp and blunt. The sharp is optional and necessary; it is permissible to slaughter with it without dislike. The blunt is permissible to slaughter with but disliked because of the delay in blood flow; thus in al-Muhit. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And it is disliked to sever the spinal cord, cut off the head, and slaughter from the back until reaching the spinal cord—which is a white thread inside the bone of the back of the neck.' 'Nakha' is the neck, with fat-ha and damm in language. Al-Nihaya said: and whoever says it is a white vein has erred. The author of al-'Inaya objected that one who names it as mentioned is not mistaken because linguists mentioned it as a thread. And it is only disliked because of the prohibition of slaughtering the sheep by breaking its neck; its explanation is what we mentioned. It is said: to stretch its head until its throat appears; it is said: to break its neck before it calms from movement; all of that is disliked. Cutting off the head involves extra pain, so it is disliked. It is disliked to drag what one intends to slaughter and to skin it before it has cooled, and it is eaten in all that because the dislike is for an extra meaning—increased pain—so it does not cause prohibition. It is disliked to slaughter it facing other than the qibla, contrary to the sunna of facing it toward the qibla, and it is eaten. And slaughter from the back of the neck involves extra pain, so it is disliked. And it is lawful if it remains alive until the vessels are cut, so death is verified by dhabh; and if it dies before the vessels are cut, it is not eaten because death occurred by something other than dhabh.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This lesson covers the recommended (mandub) and disliked (makruh) acts in slaughter. It is recommended to sharpen the blade before slaughter, based on the hadith 'Allah has prescribed excellence in everything.' This ensures a swift death and minimizes suffering. It is disliked to sharpen the blade after the animal has been laid down, as this causes additional distress. The tool should be sharp (haddah) rather than blunt (kalilah); slaughter with a blunt tool is permissible but disliked due to the delay in blood flow. Several actions are disliked: severing the spinal cord (nakh')—which means breaking the neck before death; cutting off the head; slaughtering from the back of the neck (min al-qafa); dragging the animal; skinning before the body has cooled; and facing the animal away from the qibla. These are disliked due to causing extra pain, but they do not prohibit the meat. The key principle is that as long as the prescribed vessels are cut while the animal is alive, the slaughter is valid. If the animal dies before the vessels are cut, it is carrion and not permissible.

Key Terms
النخع (al-nakh'): Severing the spinal cord or breaking the neck before the animal dies from slaughter.
النخاع (al-nukha'): A white thread-like nerve inside the vertebra of the neck (spinal cord).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason all the disliked acts in this lesson are makruh and not haram?
A) Because they are not explicitly prohibited in the Quran.
B) Because the Prophet ﷺ did them occasionally.
C) Because the dislike is due to extra pain, which does not affect the essential validity.
D) Because they are all done after the animal is dead.

Key Takeaway
Disliked acts in slaughter are makruh tanzihi and do not render the meat haram; the meat remains halal as long as the prescribed vessels are cut while the animal is alive.

Slaughter by Wounding When Normal Dhabh Is Impossible
Alternative slaughter methods when normal dhabh is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when wounding (jarrh) is permissible as a substitute for normal slaughter (dhabh) and apply the distinctions between tame and wild animals, and between different livestock.

Core Idea
When normal slaughter is impossible, wounding is a valid substitute; but the permissibility depends on the type and location of the animal.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on alternative slaughter methods. When you cannot perform the normal throat-slitting (dhabh) because the animal is out of reach or aggressive, the school permits killing by wounding (jarrh) anywhere on the body. However, the rule changes based on whether the animal was originally tame or wild, and even on whether it ran away in the city or the desert.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish tame wild animals from wild domestic animals
Identify whether the animal is originally wild but now tame (requires dhabh) or originally domestic but has gone wild (may be killed by jarrh).
2
Understand the condition of inability
Jarrh is only valid when one is unable to perform normal dhabh. This inability must be genuine, such as when the animal is out of reach or dangerous.
3
Apply Imam Muhammad's distinction for sheep
For sheep, if it runs away in the city, wounding is not allowed (because one may still catch it); if in the desert, it is allowed. For cows and camels, inability is assumed in both city and desert.
4
Recognize the ruling for attacking animals (sa'il)
An attacking animal that cannot be caught may be killed by wounding, and the meat is halal if done with intention and basmalah. This differs from Malik's view.
5
Note the evidentiary source
The author indicates a proof is narrated (ولنا ما روي), suggesting reliance on a hadith or practice, though the specific text is not quoted in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وذبح صيد استأنس وجرح نعم توحش أو تردى في بئر) الواو عاطفة على قوله وحل ذبيحة مسلم وذبح صيد يعني وحل أكل صيد استأنس بالذبح وهو الاختيارية لقدرته عليها، وحل أكل نعم توحش أو تردى بالجرح لعجزه عن الاختيارية. هذا إذ علم أنه مات من الجرح، وإن علم أنه لم يمت من الجرح لم يؤكل» فإن أشكل ذلك أكل لأن الظاهر الموت ،به، وكذا الدجاجة إذا تعلقت على شجرة وخاف موتها صارت ذكاتها بالجرح وفي الكتاب أطلق فيما توحش من النعم وكذا فيما تردى فشمل ما إذا كان في المصر والصحراء، وعن محمد أن الشاة إذا ندت في المصر لا تحل بالعقر وإن ندت في الصحراء تحل بالعقر لتحقق العجز عن الذكاة الاختيارية وفي البقر والإبل يتحقق العجز سواء ندت في المصر أو في الصحراء فتحل بالعقر. والصائل كالناد إذا كان لا يقدر على أخذه حتى لو قتله المصول عليه وهو يريد ذكاته وسمى حل أكله خلافاً لمالك . ولنا ما روي
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And a tame wild animal is slaughtered, and a wild domestic animal is wounded, or one that falls into a well.' The waw is conjunctive on his saying: 'And the slaughter of a Muslim is lawful' and the slaughter of a wild animal means: it is lawful to eat a tame wild animal by slaughter (dhabh), which is the elective method because one has power over it. And it is lawful to eat a domestic animal that has gone wild or fallen into a well by wounding (jarrh) due to inability to perform elective slaughter. This is if it is known that it died from the wound. If it is known that it did not die from the wound, it is not eaten. If that is ambiguous, it is eaten because the apparent (belief) is that death was from it. Likewise, a chicken if it gets stuck in a tree and one fears its death, its legal slaughter (dhabh) becomes by wounding. In the book, it is absolute regarding what has gone wild from livestock, and likewise what falls into a well, so it includes whether in a city or desert. According to Muhammad, if a sheep runs away in the city, it is not lawful by wounding; if it runs away in the desert, it is lawful by wounding due to confirmed inability to perform elective slaughter. For cows and camels, inability is confirmed whether they run away in city or desert, so they are lawful by wounding. And the attacker is like the runaway if one cannot catch it, such that if the one attacked kills it while intending its slaughter and says the name, its eating is lawful, contrary to Malik. And we have what is narrated...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This lesson addresses exceptional cases where normal slaughter (dhabh) is not possible. If a wild animal (like a deer) becomes tame and one can capture it, it must be slaughtered normally by cutting the throat. Conversely, if a domestic animal (like a cow or sheep) becomes wild (mutawahhash) or falls into a well and one cannot reach it for normal slaughter, it may be killed by wounding (jarrh) anywhere on the body, as long as it is done with a sharp tool and the name of Allah is recited. The wound must be fatal and cause blood to flow. If there is doubt whether death was from the wound or from something else, the animal is presumed to have died from the wound and is halal. Imam Muhammad distinguishes between a sheep that bolts in the city (where one may still be able to catch it) and in the desert (where one cannot). For cows and camels, due to their size and strength, inability is assumed even in the city, so wounding is allowed. The same applies to an attacking animal (sa'il) that one cannot catch; killing it in self-defense with the intention of slaughter and reciting the basmalah makes it halal. The Hanafi school differs from Malik on this point. The proof is based on the general permission of slaughter by wounding when unable to perform normal dhabh.

Key Terms
الجرح (al-jarrh): Wounding, i.e., killing an animal by a sharp blow to any part of the body, as an alternative to throat-slitting when one cannot perform dhabh.
الاختيارية (al-ikhtiyariyyah): The elective method of slaughter, i.e., by cutting the throat (dhabh) when one has control over the animal.
الصائل (al-sa'il): The attacking animal, e.g., a dangerous animal that charges at a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, under what condition is a sheep that has run away lawful by wounding (jarrh)?
A) If it runs away in the city
B) If it runs away in the desert
C) Both in city and desert
D) Neither in city nor desert

Key Takeaway
Wounding (jarrh) is a valid substitute for normal slaughter when one is genuinely unable to perform dhabh, but the conditions vary by animal type and location.

Prohibition of Fanged Beasts and Clawed Birds
States the general rule and lists permissible and impermissible animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating fanged beasts and clawed birds, identify the hadith evidence, and distinguish permissible exceptions such as the field crow.

Core Idea
Any fanged beast or clawed bird is haram to eat, except the field crow — the nature of the animal determines the ruling.

This lesson covers a key ruling from the Book of Slaughtered Animals: the prohibition of eating animals with fangs or claws. The Hanafi school derives this from a clear hadith and applies it broadly, but makes exceptions for non-predatory animals like the field crow. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating an animal with fangs or claws. The ruling is that it is haram (prohibited). Note that this applies to both beasts (fangs) and birds (claws).
The prohibition is absolute, covering all such animals unless an exception is proven.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas (RA) that the Prophet (SAW) forbade eating any fanged beast and any clawed bird. This is a Sahih hadith narrated by Muslim and others.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but the Hanafi school considers it sufficient for prohibition.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'nahā' (forbade) indicates prohibition. The combination 'fanged beast and clawed bird' restricts the ruling to predatory animals. 'Clawed' specifically refers to predatory birds because claws are their weapons.
The commentator defines 'sabu' (predator) as any animal that habitually snatches and kills.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Most schools agree on the general prohibition. However, there is a difference regarding the hyena: the Shafi'i school permits it, while the Hanafi school includes it as haram because it has fangs.
This ikhtilaf is based on differing interpretations of the hadith's scope.
5
Apply the ruling to exceptions
The field crow (ghurab al-zar') is permissible because it eats grain and is not predatory. In contrast, the piebald crow (abqa') that eats carrion is haram. Crows are categorized into three types based on diet.
The exception is based on the animal's nature, not its species name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الذبائح
فصل فيما يحل ولا يحل
ولا يؤكل ذو ناب ولا مخلب من سبع وطير وحل غراب الزرع لا الأبقع الذي يأكل الجيف والضبع والضب والزنبور والسلحفاة والحشرات والحمر الأهلية والبغل وحل
تقارب ولادتها لأنه يضيع ما في بطنها. الدجاجة إذا تعلقت فرماها وأصابها ينظر؛ إن كان لا يهتدي إلى منزله حل أكله لأنه عجز عن الذكاة الاختيارية وإن كان يهتدي ذكر الفقيه أبو الليث إن أصاب المذبح حل وإن أصاب غيره فعند محمد يحل وعن غيره يحل اهـ. والله تعالى أعلم .
فصل فيما يحل ولا يحل
لما ذكر أحكام الذبائح شرع في تفصيل المأكول منها وغير المأكول إذا المقصود الأصل من شرع الذبائح التوصل إلى الأكل، وقدم الذبح لأنه وسيلة الشيء تقدم عليه في الذكر. قال رحمه الله : ولا يؤكل ذو ناب ولا مخلب من سبع وطير يعني لا يحل أكل ذي ناب من سباع البهائم وذي مخلب من سباع الطير لما روى ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن أكل ذي ناب ومخلب من سبع وطير. رواه مسلم والجماعة. والسباع جمع سبع وهو كل مختطف منتهب جارح قاتل عادة والمراد بذي المخلب ما له مخلب هو سلاح وهو مفعل من الخلب وهو مزق الجلد، ويعلم بذلك أن المراد بذي مخلب هو سباع الطير لأن كل ما له مخلب وهو الظفر كما أريد به في ذي ناب من سباع البهائم لا كل ما له ناب، ولأن طبيعة هذه الأشياء مذمومة شرعاً فيخشى أن يتولد من لحمها شيء من طباعها فيحرم إكراماً لبني آدم، وهو نظير ما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال لا ترضع لكم الحمقاء فإن اللبن يغذي ويدخل في الحديث الضبع والثعلب لأن لهما ناباً وما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أباح أكلها محمول على الابتداء. ويدخل فيه الفيل أيضاً لأنه ذو ناب ولليربوع وابن عرس من سباع الهوام والرحمة والبغاث لأنهما يأكلان الجيف والرخم جمع رخمة وهو طائر أبقع يشبه النسر في الخلقة يقال له الأنوف والبغاث طائر إلى الغبرة دون الرخم بطيء الطيران ؛ كذا في الصحاح . قال : والسباع الأسد والذئب والنمر والفهد والثعلب والضبع والكلب والفيل والقرد واليربوع وابن عرس والنسور الأهلي والبري ومن الطير الصقر والباز والعقاب والنسر والشاهين .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : ( وحل غراب الزرع) لأنه يأكل الحب وليس من سباع الطير ولا من الخبائث . قال رحمه الله : (لا الأبقع الذي يأكل الجيف والضبع والضب والزنبور والسلحفاة والحشرات والحمر الأهلية والبغل يعني هذه الأشياء لا تؤكل؛ أما الغراب الأبقع فلأنه يأكل الجيف فصار كسباع الطير والغراب ثلاثة أنواع : نوع يأكل الجيف فحسب فإنه لا يؤكل، ونوع يأكل الحب فحسب فإنه يؤكل، ونوع يخلط بينهما وهو أيضاً يؤكل عند
Book of Slaughtered Animals. Section on what is permissible and not permissible. And an animal with a fang and a bird with a claw are not eaten, and the field crow is permissible, not the piebald one which eats carrion, and the hyena, the dab lizard, the wasp, the turtle, the insects, the domestic donkeys, and the mule are not permissible. And the rabbit is permissible... (continues with commentary) He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And an animal with a fang and a bird with a claw are not eaten' meaning it is not permissible to eat a beast with a fang and a bird with a claw due to what Ibn Abbas narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade eating any fanged beast and any clawed bird. Muslim and the group narrated it. 'Siba' (beasts of prey) is the plural of 'sabu' (predator) which is every ravenous, plundering, predatory, killer by nature. And by 'clawed' is meant what has a claw as a weapon. It is understood from this that by 'clawed' is meant predatory birds because everything that has a claw is a nail, as intended in 'fanged' for predatory beasts, not everything that has a fang. And because the nature of these things is blameworthy legally, it is feared that something of their nature might be generated from their flesh, so it is prohibited out of honoring the children of Adam. And it is similar to what is narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Do not let a foolish woman nurse you, for milk nourishes and enters.' The hyena and fox enter the hadith because they have fangs, and what is narrated that he permitted eating them is taken as abrogated. The elephant also enters because it has fangs, and the jerboa and weasel from the crawling predators, and the vulture and buzzard because they eat carrion. And the 'rakham' (Egyptian vulture) is a piebald bird resembling the eagle in form, called 'anuf' and 'bughath' is a dusty-colored bird slower than the vulture. So in the Sahah. He said: 'And the predators are lion, wolf, tiger, cheetah, fox, hyena, dog, elephant, monkey, jerboa, weasel, and domestic and wild vultures, and from birds: falcon, hawk, eagle, vulture, and peregrine.' He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And the field crow is permissible' because it eats grain and is not from predatory birds nor from impure things. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Not the piebald one which eats carrion, and the hyena, the dab lizard, the wasp, the turtle, the insects, the domestic donkeys, and the mule' meaning these things are not eaten. As for the piebald crow, because it eats carrion, it became like predatory birds. And crows are three types: a type that eats only carrion - it is not eaten; a type that eats only grain - it is eaten; and a type that mixes between them - it is also eaten according to...
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Slaughtered Animals (Kitab al-Dhaba'i). The author opens by stating the general rule: any animal (whether beast or bird) that has fangs or claws is not permissible to eat. This is based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly forbade eating such animals. The hadith is narrated by Muslim and other major hadith collectors. The commentator then defines 'sabu' (predator) as any animal that habitually snatches, plunders, wounds, and kills. 'Clawed' means having a claw as a weapon, which is specific to predatory birds, not all birds with claws. The wisdom behind the prohibition is that the nature of these animals is blameworthy, and it is feared that consuming their flesh may transfer some of their characteristics to humans. This is a form of honoring the children of Adam. The commentator then explains that the prohibition includes the hyena and fox because they have fangs, abrogating earlier permission. The elephant is included because it has fangs, and the jerboa and weasel are considered crawling predators. Vultures and buzzards are included because they eat carrion. The text then provides an exception: the field crow (ghurab al-zar') is permissible because it eats grain and is not predatory or impure. In contrast, the piebald crow (abqa') that eats carrion is not permissible. Crows are categorized into three types: those that eat only carrion (not eaten), those that eat only grain (eaten), and those that mix (also eaten, according to some).

Key Terms
ذو ناب (dhū nāb): An animal possessing fangs (canine teeth) used for predation, such as lion, wolf, etc.
ذو مخلب (dhū mukhlab): A bird having a claw (talon) used for gripping and killing prey, such as falcon, hawk, eagle.
سبع (saba'): A predatory animal that snatches and kills habitually, including both beasts and birds.
غراب الزرع (ghurāb al-zar'): Field crow, a type of crow that eats grain and is not a predator; thus permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is the reason for the prohibition of eating fanged beasts and clawed birds?
A) They are all najis (impure) by nature.
B) Their nature is blameworthy, and consuming their flesh may transfer harmful traits to humans.
C) They are not properly slaughtered.
D) They are harmful to the digestive system.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits all fanged beasts and clawed birds, with exceptions for non-predatory animals like the field crow, based on the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Rulings on Rabbit and Slaughter of Non-Edible Animals
Explains the permissibility of rabbit and the effect of slaughter on non-edible animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of rabbit and explain why slaughter of non-edible animals purifies the skin but does not make the meat permissible.

Core Idea
Rabbit is halal; slaughter purifies skin of non-edible animals, but meat remains impermissible for consumption.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʼiq clarifies two related issues: first, which animals are permissible to eat, focusing on the rabbit as a halal example, and second, the effect of ritual slaughter (dhakāh) on animals whose meat is not eaten. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying fiqh rules on purity and consumption. The Hanafi school holds that slaughter (dhakāh) purifies both the meat and skin of non-edible animals (except humans and pigs), while the Shafi'i school disagrees. The text also lists several animals that are forbidden due to being classified as khabāʼith (filthy things).

Guided Path
1
Identify the halal animals
Find the animal explicitly declared halal in the text. The rabbit is declared permissible because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered his companions to eat it when it was roasted. The text also notes that it is not a predator and does not eat carrion, comparing it to the deer.
The proof is a hadith from Ahmad and Nasa'i.
2
Identify the haram animals
List the animals the text declares forbidden. These include: the magpie (ʻaqʻaq), hyena, dab lizard, wasp, turtle, insects, domestic donkeys, and mules (with nuance). The basis is their classification as khabāʼith (impure things) or explicit prohibition.
The Quranic verse 7:157 (yuḥarrimu ʻalayhimu l-khabāʼith) is the general proof.
3
Understand the effect of slaughter on non-edible animals
The text explains the Hanafi position: slaughter (dhakāh) purifies the meat and skin of animals whose meat is not eaten, except for humans and pigs. This is because slaughter removes impure fluids, analogous to tanning. The skin becomes pure and can be used, even though the meat remains impermissible to eat.
The Shafi'i position is that slaughter has no effect because its primary purpose is to make meat halal.
4
Examine the proofs for the Hanafi position
The Hanafi proof is that slaughter is effective in removing impure fluids (like blood), thus purifying the skin. This is compared to tanning, which also purifies skin. The text also notes that the fat becomes pure, and the meat may be used for non-eating purposes if it becomes pure.
The act of a Zoroastrian is not considered valid for slaughter.
5
Apply the rulings to practical cases
From this text, derive that rabbit is halal. For a wolf (non-edible), if slaughtered, its skin becomes pure and usable, but its meat cannot be eaten. A pig is never purified by slaughter, and a human is never subject to slaughter.
This is the muftā bihī position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأرنب وذبح ما لا يؤكل لحمه يطهر لحمه وجلده إلا الآدمي والخنزير ولا يؤكل مائي الإمام وهو العقعق لأنه يأكل الدجاج. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يكره أكله لأنه غالب أكله الجيف والأول أصح. قال في النهاية : ذكر في بعض المواضع أن الخفاش يؤكل، وذكر في بعضها أنه لا يؤكل لأن له ناباً . وأما الضبع فلما روينا وبينا ولأنه يؤكل الجيف فيكون لحمه خبيئاً. وأما الضب والزنبور والسلحفاة والحشرات فلأنها من الخبائث وقد قال تعالى ويحرم عليكم الخبائث) [الأعراف : ١٥٧] وما روي من الإباحة محمول على ما قبل التحريم ثم حرم الخبائث لأنه لم يكن محرماً في الابتداء إلا ثلاثة أشياء على ما قال الله تعالى قل لا أجد فيما أوحي إلي محرما [الأنعام: ١٤٥] إلى آخر الآية . ثم حرم بعد ذلك أشياء . وأما الحمر الأهلية فلما روى البخاري رحمه الله تعالى حرم رسول الله له و لحوم الحمر الأهلية. وأما البغل فلأنه من نسل الحمار فكان كأصله حتى لو كانت أمه فرساً فعلى الخلاف المعروف في الخيل لأن المعتبر هو الأم. قال رحمه الله : وحل (الأرنب لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر أصحابه أن يأكلوه حين أهدي إليه مشوياً. رواه أحمد والنسائي. ولأنه ليس من السباع ولا يؤكل الجيف فأشبه الظبي. الله : ( وذبح ما لا يؤكل لحمه يطهر لحمه وجلده إلا الآدمي والخنزير) وقال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : الذكاة لا تؤثر في جميع ذلك لأن أثر الذكاة في إباحة اللحم أصل وفي طهارته وطهارة الجلد تبع ولا تبع بدون الأصل فصار نظير ذبح المجوس . ولنا أن الذكاة مؤثرة في إزالة الرطوبات النجسة فإذا زالت طهرت كما في الدباغ، وهذا الحكم مقصود في الجلد كالتناول في اللحم وفعل المجوسي غير معتد به فلا بد من الدباغ، وكما يطهر لحمه يطهر شحمه أيضاً حتى لو وقع في الماء القليل لا يفسده. وهل يجوز الانتفاع به لغير الأكل؟ قيل لا يجوز اعتباراً بالأكل، وقيل يجوز كالزيت إذا خالطه شحم الميتة والزيت غالب فإنه ينتفع به في غير الأكل. والخنزير لا يؤثر فيه الدباغ لنجاستة والآدمي لكرامته وفي روايتة لا يطهر بالذكاة لحم ما لا يؤكل لحمه والجلد يطهر هو الصحيح وقد مر في كتاب الطهارة اهـ
The rabbit, and slaughtering an animal whose meat is not eaten purifies its meat and skin except for humans and pigs. And the 'mai al-imam' which is the magpie is not eaten because it eats chicken. And from Abu Yusuf that he dislikes eating it because most of its food is carrion, and the first is more correct. Al-Nihaya said: It is mentioned in some places that bat is eaten, and in others that it is not eaten because it has fangs. As for the hyena, based on what we narrated and made clear, and because it eats carrion, its flesh becomes impure. As for the dab lizard, wasp, turtle, and insects, they are from the impure things, and Allah said 'And He prohibits for you the impure things' (Al-A'raf 157). And what is narrated of permissibility is taken as before prohibition; then He prohibited impure things because initially only three things were prohibited as Allah said 'Say: I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden' (Al-An'am 145) to the end of the verse. Then later Allah prohibited other things. As for domestic donkeys, because Bukhari narrated that the Messenger of Allah prohibited their flesh and milk. As for the mule, because it is from the progeny of the donkey, so it is like its origin, so if its mother is a horse, it follows the known disagreement about horses because consideration is given to the mother. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And the rabbit is permissible' because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered his companions to eat it when it was gifted to him roasted. Ahmad and Nasa'i narrated it. And because it is not from predators nor eats carrion, so it resembles the deer. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And slaughtering an animal whose meat is not eaten purifies its meat and skin except for humans and pigs.' Al-Shafi'i said: Slaughtering has no effect on all that because the effect of slaughter is primarily on making meat permissible, and on its purity and the purity of the skin as a subsidiary, and there is no subsidiary without the original, so it becomes like slaughtering of a Zoroastrian. Our proof: slaughter is effective in removing impure fluids, so if they are removed, it becomes pure, as in tanning. This ruling is intended for the skin just as consumption is for the meat. The act of a Zoroastrian is not considered, so tanning is still needed. And just as its meat becomes pure, so does its fat; even if it falls into a small amount of water, it does not spoil it. Is it permissible to benefit from it for other than eating? Some say no, by analogy with eating; some say yes, like oil if it is mixed with the fat of a dead animal and the oil is predominant, then it is used for other than eating. The pig is not affected by tanning due to its impurity, and humans due to their honor. In another narration, slaughter does not purify the meat of an animal whose meat is not eaten, but the skin is purified; that is the correct opinion, and it has passed in the Book of Purity. End.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage contains multiple rulings: (1) Rabbit is halal based on the Prophet's order to eat it when presented roasted, and because it is not a predator and does not eat carrion. (2) The magpie ('aq' aq) is not eaten because it eats chicken (or carrion according to Abu Yusuf). (3) The hyena is not eaten due to eating carrion. (4) Others like dab lizard, wasp, turtle, insects are forbidden as 'khaba'ith' (impure things). (5) Domestic donkeys are forbidden by the Prophet. (6) Mules are forbidden, but if the mother is a horse, there is a disagreement; the mother's species decides. Then the text discusses the effect of slaughter on animals whose meat is not eaten: slaughter purifies both meat and skin, except for humans and pigs. This is a Hanafi position; Shafi'i disagrees, saying slaughter has no effect. The Hanafi proof is that slaughter removes impure fluids, similar to tanning. This ruling is intended for the skin to be used, and the same applies to fat. The meat may be used for non-eating purposes if it becomes pure. The pig and human are exceptions: pig is not purified by slaughter, and humans are not subject to slaughter. The correct opinion according to the text is that the skin is purified even if the meat is not considered pure for eating. This was already covered in the Book of Purity.

Key Terms
خبائث (khabā'ith): Things considered impure or filthy, which are prohibited by the Quranic verse.
ذكاة (dhakāh): The ritual slaughter that causes the animal to become permissible for eating and purifies the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of slaughtering a wolf (a non-edible animal)?
A) The meat becomes halal and the skin becomes pure.
B) The meat remains impure but becomes spiritually pure; the skin becomes pure.
C) The meat and skin both become pure, but the meat is not permissible to eat.
D) Neither the meat nor the skin is affected.

Key Takeaway
Rabbit is halal; slaughter purifies the skin of non-edible animals, making it usable, but the meat remains impermissible to eat.

The Hanafi Ruling on Non-Fish Seafood
Inter-madhhab dispute on permissibility of non-fish sea creatures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on non-fish aquatic animals, identify the opposing Shafi'i and Maliki positions, and explain the Hanafi proof from Quran 7:157 and the hadith of the whale.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, only fish is halal; all other sea creatures are haram as khabā'ith.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute about seafood. While many assume all sea creatures are permissible, the Hanafi school takes a restricted view, permitting only fish. Understanding the evidence behind each position sharpens your ability to weigh proofs and appreciate madhhab methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: only fish is halal; all other aquatic animals are prohibited (khabā'ith).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shāfi‘ī permits all sea creatures; Imam Mālik permits most except certain ones (pig, predators, dog, human).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They cite Qur’ān 5:96 (‘Lawful to you is the game of the sea’) as general permission, and a hadith about a stranded whale (al-‘Anbar) that they ate.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school specifies the general verse with Qur’ān 7:157 prohibiting ‘khabā’ith’, and treats the whale hadith as a specific exception for fish, not a general rule.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, Hanafis avoid shrimp, lobster, crab, etc., while Shāfi‘īs and Mālikīs generally consume them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولا يؤكل مائي السمك غير (طاف وقال مالك رحمه الله تعالى : يؤكل جميع حيوان الماء واستثنى بعضهم الخنزير والسباع والكلب والآدمي، وعن الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى أباح ذلك كله. وقال صاحب الهداية : والخلاف في الأكل والبيع واحد وينبغي بيعه بالإجماع لطهارته . لهم قوله تعالى أحل لكم صيد البحر ﴾ [المائدة: ٩٦] من غير فصل ولأنه لا دم في هذه الأشياء لأن الدموي لا يسكن الماء والمحرم هو الدم فأشبه السمك. وروى جابر أنهم أصابهم جوع شديد في الغزو فألقى البحر حوتاً ميتاً يقال له العنير فأكلنا منه نصف شهر قال : فلما قدمنا المدينة وذكر ذلك لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : كلوا رزقاً أخرجه الله لكم أطعمونا إن كان معكم الحديث. ولنا قوله تعالى ويحرم عليهم الخبائث [الأعراف : ١٥٧] وهذا منها . قال في النهاية : إن كراهة الخبائث تحريمية وما سوى السمك
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And the aquatic animal other than fish is not eaten.' And Malik said: 'All water animals are eaten, except some exclude pig, predators, dog, and human.' And from al-Shafi'i, he permitted all of that. The author of al-Hidayah said: 'The disagreement regarding eating and selling is the same, and it should be sold by consensus due to its purity.' Their proof is Allah's statement 'Lawful to you is the game of the sea' (Al-Ma'ida 96) without distinction, and because there is no blood in these things because a blood-bearing animal does not reside in water, and what is prohibited is blood, so it resembles fish. And Jabir narrated: A severe hunger struck them during an expedition, so the sea threw up a dead fish called 'al-‘Anbar' and they ate from it for half a month. He said: When we came to Madinah and mentioned that to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), he said: 'Eat, it is provision Allah brought out for you; feed us if you have any' the hadith. Our proof is Allah's statement 'And He prohibits for them the impure things' (Al-A'raf 157), and this is from them. Al-Nihaya said: Indeed, the prohibition of impure things is for prohibition (tahrim), and other than fish [is prohibited].
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute about the permissibility of eating aquatic animals other than fish. The Hanafi position is that only fish are halal; all other sea creatures (like crabs, lobsters, etc.) are not eaten and are considered impure (khaba'ith). Imam Malik permits all water animals except some like pig, predators, dog, and human. Imam Shafi'i permits all sea creatures without exception. The Hanafi proof includes the Quranic verse that prohibits 'khaba'ith' (impure things), and these creatures are considered impure. Additionally, they argue that the hadith about the dead fish 'al-‘Anbar' (whale/ambergris) is an exception. The opponents' proof is the general verse 'Lawful to you is the game of the sea' (Al-Ma'ida 96) which includes all sea animals, and the argument that there is no blood in these creatures, so they resemble fish. The Hanafi response is that the general verse is specified by the verse on khaba'ith. The text from al-Nihaya confirms that the prohibition is tahrimi (not just makruh). The ruling extends to selling: it should be sold by consensus because it is pure (since it came from water), but eating is not allowed.

Key Terms
مائي السمك (mā'ī al-samak): Literally 'water fish', meaning aquatic animals that are not fish; i.e., all sea creatures besides fish.
خبائث (khabā'ith): Impure things; in this context, non-fish aquatic animals are considered khabā'ith and prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on eating shrimp?
A) Permissible because it comes from the sea.
B) Not permissible because it is not a fish and is considered khabā'ith.
C) Permissible only if slaughtered.
D) Permissible but makruh.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, only fish is halal; all other aquatic animals are haram as khabā'ith.

Definition and Conditions of Udhiyya
Introduces the book of sacrifice: definitions and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define udhiyya in linguistic and technical terms, distinguish its conditions of obligation from conditions of performance, and identify the two types of financial worship combined in udhiyya.

Core Idea
Udhiyya is a specific sacrifice combining bloodshed (destruction) and meat charity (possession), obligatory on the wealthy resident Muslim.

This passage opens the Book of Udhiyya in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author first explains why this chapter follows the chapter on slaughterings, then provides both linguistic and juristic definitions of udhiyya. He distinguishes between conditions for obligation (being a wealthy resident Muslim) and conditions for performance (time, place, cause, pillar). He concludes by noting that udhiyya uniquely combines two forms of financial worship: destruction (bloodshed) and possession (charity of meat).

Guided Path
1
Understand the placement of the chapter
The author justifies placing udhiyya after general slaughter (dhaba'ih) because udhiyya is a specific type of slaughter; the specific follows the general.
This is a common organizational principle in fiqh works.
2
Define udhiyya linguistically
According to al-Nihaya, udhiyya is a sheep sacrificed on the Day of Sacrifice. Note alternative plurals and forms (adāḥī, ḍaḥāyā, aḍḥā).
The definition from al-Qamus and al-Sihah is similar, though they omit the word 'sacrificed' – the author explains it is implied.
3
Define udhiyya technically
According to jurists, udhiyya is the name for a specific animal—the four types (camel, cow, sheep, goat) and the jadh'a of sheep—slaughtered with the intention of drawing near to Allah on a specific day (the days of Nahr).
The jadh'a is a one-year-old sheep, specifically mentioned as acceptable.
4
Identify conditions of obligation
The conditions for udhiyya being obligatory are: being a resident (muqim), wealthy (musir), from civilised areas (cities, villages, deserts), and Muslim. Puberty and sanity are not required; a guardian may sacrifice for a minor or insane person if they have wealth.
'Musir' is defined as having the nisab of zakat or the amount for sadaqat al-fitr, beyond basic needs.
5
Identify conditions of performance
For the sacrifice to be valid, the time must be after the Eid prayer for urban residents (the place of the sacrificer is the reference), the cause is the dawn of the day of Nahr, and the pillar is slaughtering a permissible animal with the proper intention.
The author will discuss the description (sifa) of the slaughter later.
6
Recognize the dual nature of udhiyya
Financial acts of worship are of two types: those involving possession (e.g., charity) and those involving destruction (e.g., manumission). Udhiyya combines both: the bloodshed is destruction, and the charity of meat is possession.
This combination makes udhiyya unique among acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية
أورده عقب الذبائح لأنها ذبيحة خاصة والذبائح عام والخاص بعد العام، وتعقب لأنه بأنهم إن أرادوا أن الخاص بعد العام في الوجود فهو ممنوع أن لا تقرر وجود للعام إلا في ضمن الخاص وإن أرادوا في التعقل فهو إنما يكون إذا كان العام ذاتياً للخاص وكان الخاص معقولاً كما عرف وكون الأمر كذلك فيما نحن فيه ممنوع. ويمكن أن يقال : تميز الذاتي من العرضي إنما يتعسر في الحقائق النفسانية، وأما في الأمور الوضيعة والاعتبارية كما نحن فيه فكل من اعتبر داخلاً في مفهوم شيء يكون ذاتياً له ويكون تصور ذلك الشيء تصوراً له بالكلية، ولا شك أن معنى الذبح داخل في معنى الأضحية فتوقف تعقلها على تعقل معنى الذبح فيتم التعريف على اختيار الشق الثاني. وهو في اللغة كما في النهاية شاة نحرها تذبح في يوم الأضحية ولا يخالفه ما في القاموس والصحاح من أنها شاة من غير لفظ نحرها لأن لفظ النحر مراد بدليل الأضحية. وتجمع على أضاحي بالتشديد ويقال أضحية وضحايا كهدية وهدايا. ويقال أضحاة وتجمع على الشاة فصاعداً أضحى. وعند الفقهاء كما في النهاية اسم الحيوان مخصوص وهي الأنواع الأربعة والجذع من الضأن تذبح بنية القربة في يوم مخصوص اهـ. ولها شرائط وجوب وشرائط آداء ،وصفة فالأول كونه مقيماً موسراً من أهل الأمصار والقرى والبوادي والإسلام ،شرط وأما البلوغ والعقل فليسا بشرط حتى لو كان للصغير والمجنون مال فإنه يضحي عنه أبوه وأما شرائط آدابها فمنها الوقت في حق المصري بعد صلاة الإمام والمعتبر مكان الأضحية لا مكان المضحي وسببها طلوع فجر يوم النحر، وركنها ذبح ما يجوز ذبحه وسيأتي الكلام في صفتها. واعلم أن القربة المالية نوعان : نوع بطريق التمليك كالصدقات ونوع بطريق الإتلاف كالإعتاق والأضحية، وفي الأضحية اجتمع المعنيان فإنه يتقرب بإراقة الدم وهو إتلاف ثم بالتصدق باللحم فيكون تمليكاً اهـ.
Book of Sacrifice. It is placed after the chapter on slaughterings because it is a specific sacrifice and slaughterings are general, and the specific comes after the general. This is questioned because if they mean that the specific comes after the general in existence, it is not accepted that the general exists except within the specific; if they mean in conceptualization, that only occurs when the general is essential to the specific and the specific is conceptualized as known, and that being the case here is not accepted. It could be said: distinguishing the essential from the accidental is difficult in psychological realities, but in artificial and conventional matters as we have here, anything considered part of the concept of something is essential to it, and conceptualizing that thing is complete conceptualization of it. There is no doubt that the meaning of slaughter is part of the meaning of udhiyya, so its conceptualization depends on conceptualizing the meaning of slaughter; thus the definition is complete on the second interpretation. In language, as in al-Nihaya, it is a sheep sacrificed by cutting the throat on the day of udhiyya. This is not contradicted by al-Qamus and al-Sihah's definition that it is a sheep without mentioning the slaughter, because the word slaughter is intended, as evidenced by udhiyya. It is pluralized as adāḥī with tashdīd, and also uḍḥiyya and ḍaḥāyā like hadiyya and hadāyā. It is also said uḍḥāh and pluralized for one sheep or more as aḍḥā. According to jurists, as in al-Nihaya, it is the name for a specific animal—the four types and the jadh‘a of sheep—slaughtered with the intention of drawing near to Allah on a specific day. It has conditions for obligation and conditions for performance, and a description. The first: being a resident, wealthy, from the people of cities, villages, and deserts, and Islam is a condition. Puberty and sanity are not conditions, so if a minor or an insane person has wealth, his father sacrifices on his behalf. Among conditions for performance: time—for the urban resident, after the imam’s prayer; the place considered is the place of sacrifice, not the place of the sacrificer; its cause is the dawn of the day of Nahr; its pillar is slaughtering what is permissible to slaughter; its description will be discussed later. Know that financial acts of drawing near are of two types: one by way of possession like charities, and one by way of destruction like manumission and udhiyya. In udhiyya, both meanings are combined: one draws near by spilling blood—which is destruction—then by giving the meat in charity—which is possession.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the Book of Udhiyya (Sacrifice) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The author first justifies placing this chapter after the chapter on slaughterings (Dhaba'ih) by explaining that udhiyya is a specific type of slaughter, and the specific follows the general. He then addresses a potential objection about the relationship between the general and specific, concluding that in conventional matters, any element included in the concept is essential, so the meaning of slaughter is essential to udhiyya. Next, he provides the linguistic definition from al-Nihaya: udhiyya is a sheep sacrificed on the Day of Sacrifice (Yawm al-Adha). He notes alternative plurals and forms. The technical definition according to jurists is that it is the name for a specific animal—the four types (camel, cow, sheep, goat) and the jadh'a (one-year-old) of sheep—slaughtered with the intention of drawing near to Allah on a specific day (the days of Nahr). He then lists the conditions for obligation: being a resident, wealthy, from urban or rural areas, and being Muslim. Puberty and sanity are not required; a guardian may sacrifice on behalf of a minor or insane person if they have wealth. Conditions for performance include the time (after the imam's prayer for urban residents) and the place (the place of sacrifice, not the sacrificer's location). The cause is the dawn of the day of Nahr. The pillar is slaughtering what is permissible. The author also explains that financial acts of devotion are of two types: those involving transfer of ownership (like charity) and those involving destruction (like manumission). Udhiyya combines both: the bloodshed is destruction, and giving the meat in charity is transfer of ownership.

Key Terms
الأضحية (al-udhiyya): In this text, it refers to the animal sacrificed specifically on the days of Nahr (10-12 Dhu al-Hijjah) as an act of worship, combining the meanings of destruction (bloodshed) and possession (charity of meat).
الموسر (al-musir): Literally 'wealthy'; here it refers to one who possesses the nisab of zakat or the amount required for sadaqat al-fitr, as a condition for obligation of udhiyya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the obligation of udhiyya?
A) Being a resident (muqim)
B) Being wealthy (musir)
C) Being an adult (baligh)
D) Being Muslim

Key Takeaway
Udhiyya is a worship combining destruction and charity, obligatory on the wealthy resident Muslim, with specific conditions for its performance.

The Obligation of Udhiyya: Wajib or Sunnah?
State ruling, present dispute, give proofs, discuss conditions and capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on udhiyya, identify the evidence for wajib vs sunnah views, and distinguish between possible capacity (qudra mumkina) and easy capacity (qudra muyassara).

Core Idea
Udhiyya is wajib upon the wealthy resident – a severe threat proves it is no mere sunnah.

The sacrifice of udhiyya during Eid al-Adha is a central act of worship, but its legal status is debated even within the Hanafi school. In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author presents the ruling according to the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq): it is wajib upon a free, Muslim, wealthy resident. He then lays out the dissenting views of the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i, who consider it sunnah, and provides the proof texts for both sides. Understanding this dispute is crucial for knowing the binding force of the act and the consequences of omitting it.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its conditions
Identify the ruling as stated: wajib upon a free, Muslim, wealthy resident, for himself, with one sheep or 1/7 camel, from Fajr of 10th Dhu al-Hijjah to end of Tashriq. Note the conditions (shurut) that restrict obligation.
The passage explicitly lists 'free, Muslim, wealthy, resident' – each condition is explained later.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa says wajib; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say sunnah (aligned with al-Shafi'i). Locate where the author reports this.
Al-Tahawi is cited as reporting the sunnah view from both Sahibayn.
3
Analyse the proofs for the sunnah view
Two proofs: (1) Hadith: 'When you see the crescent... and one of you intends to sacrifice...' – condition of intention negates obligation. (2) Qiyas: If wajib on resident, it would be wajib on traveler like zakat and fitr – but it is not, so it cannot be wajib.
The author presents these fairly before responding.
4
Analyse the proofs for the wajib view
Two proofs: (1) Hadith: 'Whoever finds capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place' – severe threat only for obligation. (2) Command to repeat sacrifice if done before prayer – implies repetition is required, hence obligation. Also rebuttal of the travel analogy: traveler excused due to hardship, unlike zakat that doesn't expire.
The author uses these to defend the matn's position.
5
Examine the capacity type (qudra mumkina vs muyassara)
The passage argues obligation is based on possible capacity (having means at start) not easy capacity (ongoing). Evidence: if wealthy buys sheep then becomes poor after days, he must still give charity – obligation does not drop. Contrast with zakat where nisab must persist.
This refined point determines whether poverty after the days affects the duty.

Source Text

تجب على حر مسلم موسر مقیم على نفسه لا عن طفلة شاة أو سبع بدنة فجر يوم النحر إلى آخر أيامه يعني صفتها أنها واجبة وعن أبي يوسف أنها سنة. وذكر الطحاوي أنها سنة على قول أبي يوسف ومحمد وهو قول الشافعي؛ لهم قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «إذا رأيتم هلال ذي الحجة وأراد أحدكم أن يضحي فليمسك عن شعره وأظفاره»(۱) رواه مسلم وجماعة أخر والتعليق بالإرادة ينافي الوجوب ولأنها لو كانت واجبة على المقيم لوجبت على المسافر كالزكاة وصدقة الفطر لأنهما لا يختلفان بالعبادة المالية. ودليل الوجوب قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم من وجد سعة ولم يضح فلا يقربن مصلانا (۲) رواه أحمد وابن ماجه. ومثل هذا الوعيد لا يلحق بترك غير الواجب، ولأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر بإعادتها من قوله «من ضحى قبل الصلاة فليعد الأضحية» (۳) وإنما لا تجب على المسافر لأن أداءها مختص بأسباب تشق على المسافر وتفوت بمضي الوقت فلا يجب عليه شيء لدفع الحرج عنه كالجمعة بخلاف الزكاة وصدقة الفطر لأنهما لا يفوتان بمضي الزمان فلا يخرج. وأما العتيرة فذبيحة تذبح في رجب يتقرب بها أهل الجاهلية والإسلام في الصدر الأول ثم نسخ في الإسلام؛ كذا في الأصل. وفي المحيط : لو اشترى الفقير شاة فضحى بها ثم أيسر في آخر أيام النحر قيل عليه أن يعيدها وقيل لا ولو افتقر في أيام النحر سقطت عنه، كذا لو مات ولو بعدها لم تسقط ؛ كذا في المحيط قيد بالحر لأنه عبادة مالية فلا تجب على العبد لأنه لا يملك ولو ملك، وبالإسلام لأنها عبادة والكافر ليس بأهل لها، وباليسار لأنها لا تجب إلا على القادر وهو الغني دون الفقير ومقداره مقدار ما تجب فيه صدقة الفطر وقد مر بيانه . قال في العناية أخذاً من النهاية وهي واجبة بالقدرة الممكنة بدليل أن الموسر إذا اشترى شاة للأضحية في أول يوم النحر ولم يضح حتى مضت أيام النحر ثم افتقر كان عليه أن يتصدق بعينها أو بقيمتها ولا تسقط عنه الأضحية ، فلو كانت بالقدرة الميسرة لكان دوامها شرطاً كما في الزكاة والعشر والخراج حيث يسقط بهلاك النصاب والخارج واصطلام الزرع آفة لا يقال أدنى ما يتمكن به المرء من إقامتها تملك قيمة ما يصلح للأضحية ولم يجب إلا بملك النصاب فدل أن وجوبها بالقدرة الميسرة لأن اشتراط النصاب لا ينافي وجوبها بالممكنة كما في صدقة الفطر، وهذا لأنها وظيفة مالية نظراً إلى شرطها وهو الحرية فيشترط فيها الغنى كما في صدقة الفطر ؛ لا يقال لو كان كذلك لوجب التمليك وليس كذلك لأن
It is obligatory upon a free, Muslim, wealthy, resident person on his own behalf, not on behalf of a child: one sheep or one-seventh of a camel, from the dawn of the day of Nahr until the end of its days. That is, its description is that it is wajib. And from Abu Yusuf it is sunnah. Al-Tahawi mentioned that it is sunnah according to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is the view of al-Shafi'i. Their evidence: the Prophet ﷺ said, 'When you see the crescent of Dhu al-Hijjah and one of you intends to sacrifice, let him refrain from his hair and nails' – reported by Muslim and others. The suspension upon intention negates obligation. And because if it were obligatory upon the resident, it would be obligatory upon the traveler like zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, as they do not differ regarding financial worship. The evidence for obligation: the Prophet ﷺ said, 'Whoever finds capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place' – reported by Ahmad and Ibn Majah. Such a warning does not attach to abandoning a non-obligatory act. And because he ﷺ commanded its repetition in his words, 'Whoever sacrifices before the prayer, let him repeat the sacrifice.' And it is not obligatory upon the traveler because its performance is specific to causes that are difficult for the traveler and are lost with the passage of time, so nothing is obligatory upon him to remove hardship, like Jumu'ah, unlike zakat and sadaqat al-fitr because they are not lost with the passage of time, so they are not excused. As for al-‘atira, it is a sacrifice slaughtered in Rajab by which the people of Jahiliyyah and early Islam sought proximity, then it was abrogated in Islam; thus in al-Asl. In al-Muhit: If a poor person bought a sheep and sacrificed with it, then became wealthy at the end of the days of Nahr, there is a saying that he must repeat it, and another that he need not. If he became poor during the days of Nahr, it is dropped from him; likewise if he died, and if after them it is not dropped; thus in al-Muhit. He qualified with 'free' because it is a financial act of worship, so it is not obligatory upon a slave because he does not own property even if he owns. And with 'Muslim' because it is worship and the disbeliever is not qualified for it. And with 'wealthy' because it is only obligatory upon the capable, who is the rich, not the poor; its measure is the measure of what makes sadaqat al-fitr obligatory, and its explanation has passed. He said in al-‘Inaya, taking from al-Nihaya: It is obligatory with possible capacity (al-qudra al-mumkina), evidenced by the fact that if a wealthy person bought a sheep for sacrifice on the first day of Nahr and did not sacrifice until the days of Nahr passed, then became poor, he must give charity with the sheep itself or its value, and the sacrifice is not dropped from him. If it were with easy capacity (al-qudra al-muyassara), its continuity would be a condition as in zakat, 'ushr, and kharaj, where they drop with the destruction of the nisab and the produce and the blight of the crop. It cannot be said that the minimum by which one can perform it is owning the value of what is suitable for sacrifice, and it is not obligatory except with ownership of nisab, so it indicates that its obligation is with possible capacity, because conditioning nisab does not negate its obligation with possible capacity as in sadaqat al-fitr. This is because it is a financial function considering its condition which is freedom, so wealth is required in it as in sadaqat al-fitr. It cannot be said: if so, possession would be obligatory, and it is not so, because...
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the central dispute regarding the legal status of the sacrificial animal (udhiyya). The author begins by stating the ruling as found in the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq): it is obligatory (wajib) upon a free, Muslim, wealthy, resident person, for himself (not on behalf of a child), consisting of one sheep or one-seventh of a camel (or cow), from the dawn of the day of Nahr (10th Dhu al-Hijjah) until the end of the days of Tashriq (13th). He immediately notes the intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds it is sunnah, and al-Tahawi reports that both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider it sunnah, aligning with Imam al-Shafi'i. The author then presents the evidence for the sunnah view: (1) The hadith 'When you see the crescent... and one of you intends to sacrifice...' – the condition of intention (irada) indicates that it is not obligatory, as obligation does not depend on intention. (2) Analogy: If it were wajib on the resident, it would also be wajib on the traveler, like zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, which are financial obligations that do not differ between resident and traveler. Next, the author provides the evidence for obligation (wajib): (1) The hadith 'Whoever finds capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place' – such a severe threat is not attached to abandoning a recommended act. (2) The command to repeat the sacrifice if performed before the Eid prayer, which indicates that the first attempt was invalid and must be made up, implying obligation. (3) The response to the analogy with travel: udhiyya is tied to specific days and acts that are difficult for a traveler, so it is waived to avoid hardship, similar to Jumu'ah. Unlike zakat and fitr, which do not expire with time, udhiyya's time is limited. The author then discusses the conditions: 'free' because a slave cannot own even if permitted; 'Muslim' because worship is not valid from a disbeliever; 'wealthy' – the threshold is the same as sadaqat al-fitr. He cites al-‘Inaya and al-Nihaya to clarify the type of capacity required: it is 'possible capacity' (al-qudra al-mumkina), not 'easy capacity' (al-muyassara). This means that once the obligation is triggered by owning the nisab at the beginning of the time, it does not drop if wealth is later lost. The evidence is that if a wealthy person buys a sheep and then becomes poor after the days, he must still give charity with its value. If it were based on easy capacity (i.e., condition of ongoing wealth), it would drop with poverty, but it does not. The passage ends mid-sentence with a rebuttal to an objection.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): Here, wujub refers to the legal obligation (fard/wajib) of udhiyya according to Abu Hanifa, based on hadith evidence. The text distinguishes between wajib (obligatory) and sunnah (recommended).
المقيم (al-muqim): Resident as opposed to traveler (musafir). Udhiyya is only obligatory upon residents because travelers are excused due to hardship and time constraints.
القدرة الممكنة (al-qudra al-mumkina): Possible capacity: the type of ability that triggers obligation based on having the means at the start of the time, regardless of later loss. Contrasted with al-qudra al-muyassara (easy capacity) which requires ongoing ability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the main evidence Abu Hanifa uses to prove that udhiyya is wajib?
A) The hadith about refraining from hair and nails when intending to sacrifice
B) Analogy with zakat and sadaqat al-fitr
C) The hadith 'Whoever finds capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place'
D) The practice of the early Muslims (al-‘atira)

Key Takeaway
Udhiyya is wajib upon the free, Muslim, wealthy resident; omission without excuse is a sin requiring compensation in charity.

The Dual Nature of Sacrifice: Itlaf and Tamlik
Defines sacrifice's dual nature via financial worship concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why udhiyah is considered a financial act of worship and distinguish between its two components: itlaf (destruction) and tamlik (granting ownership).

Core Idea
Sacrifice combines two financial acts: destroying the animal and giving meat as charity.

In the Hanafi school, udhiyah is not just a physical act but a financial act of drawing near to Allah. This lesson explores its dual nature as explained by the commentator Al-'Inayah. Understanding this helps clarify the integral role of charity in the sacrifice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category
Recognize that the passage classifies udhiyah as al-qurb al-maliyyah (financial acts of drawing near) and that it can involve itlaf (destruction) and tamlik (granting ownership).
This is a Hanafi classification distinguishing financial worship from physical worship.
2
Analyze the two elements
Note that udhiyah involves itlaf through slaughter and tamlik through giving meat in charity. If the meat is given as charity, both are achieved; if not, only itlaf occurs.
The passage quotes Al-'Inayah to make this distinction.
3
Apply the concept
Consider how the ruling on udhiyah rests on this dual nature: the obligation is fulfilled even if one does not give charity, but the act is more complete when charity is given.
This has implications for the reward and perfection of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القرب المالية قد تحصل بالإتلاف كالإعتاق والمضحي إذا تصد باللحكم فقد حصل النوعان أعني التمليك والإتلاف بإراقة الدم، وإن لم يتصدق حصل الأخير إلى هنا لفظ العناية. وفي المحيط : لو زكى نصابه ثم عليه أيام النحر ونصابه ناقص عليه الأضحية ولا يعد فقيراً بأداء الزكاة في هذ السنة لأن قدر المؤدي يعد قائماً شرعاً، ولو انتقص في أيام النحر بغير الزكاة سقطت عنه الأضحية لأن المؤدي لا يعد قائماً حكماً فيعد فقيراً.
Financial acts of drawing near may occur through destruction, like manumission. The sacrificer, if he gives the meat in charity, has achieved both types: transferring ownership and destruction by spilling blood; and if he does not give charity, only the latter is achieved. Up to here is the wording of Al-'Inayah. And in Al-Muhit: If one paid zakat on his nisab, then during the days of sacrifice his nisab is deficient, yet sacrifice is obligatory upon him, and he is not considered poor by having paid zakat in this year, because the amount paid is considered legally present. But if the deficiency occurred during the days of sacrifice due to something other than zakat, the obligation of sacrifice drops from him, because the paid amount is not considered present legally, so he is considered poor.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of udhiyah as a financial act of worship, explaining that it combines two elements: tamlik (granting ownership) through charity and itlaf (destruction) through slaughter. If the meat is given as charity, both are achieved; otherwise only itlaf. This is from the commentary of Al-'Inayah. Then it discusses a ruling from Al-Muhit: if someone pays zakat on his nisab during the year, and during the days of sacrifice his wealth falls below nisab, he still must perform udhiyah because the paid zakat is considered legally present. However, if the deficiency is due to other spending, he is considered poor and the obligation drops. This is based on legal reasoning: the zakat paid is treated as still owned for the purpose of determining wealth.

Key Terms
القرب المالية (al-qurb al-maliyyah): Financial acts of drawing near to Allah, such as sadaqah and sacrifice.
الإتلاف (al-itlaf): Destruction of property, here through slaughter of the animal.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Granting ownership, here by giving meat as charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the sacrificer achieve if he does not give the meat in charity?
A) Both tamlik and itlaf
B) Only itlaf
C) Only tamlik
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Udhiyah in the Hanafi school is a financial act of worship that combines destruction (itlaf) and charity (tamlik).

Effect of Zakat on Nisab for Udhiyah Obligation
Ruling on how zakat affects nisab for udhiyah obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that zakat paid is considered legally present (qā'im sharʿan) and determine when a deficiency in wealth exempts one from udhiyah.

Core Idea
Zakat paid is treated as still present for nisab; other spending is not.

The obligation of udhiyah depends on possessing nisab during the days of sacrifice. But what if you pay zakat and your wealth dips? This lesson explains the key distinction from Al-Muhit: zakat is considered legally present, so you remain obligated, whereas other spending reduces your wealth and exempts you.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage presents two cases: (1) paying zakat causes a deficiency in nisab during the days of sacrifice, and (2) other spending causes the deficiency.
The ruling is from Al-Muhit.
2
Apply the legal presumption
For zakat, the paid amount is considered legally present (qa'im shar'an), so the nisab is still intact.
This is a legal fiction (hukman) used to avoid discouraging zakat.
3
Distinguish from other spending
If the deficiency is due to non-zakat expenditure, the amount spent is not considered present, so the person is considered poor and udhiyah drops.
The difference hinges on the nature of the expenditure.
4
Derive the ruling
Thus, paying zakat does not exempt from udhiyah; other spending does.
This is a unique Hanafi position emphasizing the importance of zakat.

Source Text

يعد قائماً شرعاً (yu'addu qa'iman shar'an): Considered legally present; a legal fiction that the amount paid is still regarded as owned for certain rulings.

B) Not obligatory, because his actual wealth is deficient
C) Recommended
D) Permissible

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, zakat paid is treated as still present for determining nisab for udhiyah, unlike other expenditures.

Sacrificing from a Minor's Wealth: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains obligation for self and dispute on sacrificing from child's wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding sacrificing from a minor's wealth and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits using the child's wealth; Muhammad and Zafar require the father's own due to itlaf.

The passage establishes that udhiyah is obligatory for the adult but not for the child, as it is a pure act of worship. It then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: may a father sacrifice for his minor child using the child's own wealth? This lesson unpacks the positions, proofs, and the relied-upon ruling in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is udhiyah (sacrifice) performed by a father for his minor child. The dispute is whether the father may use the child's wealth to procure the animal.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that if the child has wealth, the father may sacrifice from it, as he has guardianship (wilaya) over the child's property.
This is an extension of the father's authority in financial matters.
3
State the opposing position
Muhammad, Zafar, and al-Shafi'i argue that the sacrifice must come from the father's own wealth.
They draw an analogy (qiyas) with manumission.
4
Analyze the opposing proof
Their qiyas: manumission of a child's slave is invalid because it involves destruction (itlaf) of the child's property without ownership. Similarly, slaughtering a sacrificial animal is destruction, so the father cannot destroy what he does not own.
The illa (effective cause) is itlaf combined with lack of ownership.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical consequence: if a father sacrifices from the child's wealth, the validity is contested, and he may be liable for compensation (daman). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from Al-Kafi is that the father should not use the child's wealth.
Al-Hidayah also states the most correct is from the father's own wealth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذا مر عن يعني لا يجب عليه وقوله عن نفسه لأنه أصل في الوجوب عليه وقوله «لا طفله عن أولاده الصغار لأنها عبادة محضة بخلاف صدقة الفطر، والأول ظاهر الرواية. وإن كان للصغير مال يضحي عنه أبوه ماله أو من ماله عند أبي حنيفة. وقال محمد وزفر وصيه من والشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : من مال الأب لأن الإراقة إتلاف والأب لا يملكه في مال الصغير كالإعتاق، والأصح أنه يضحي من ماله ويأكل منه ما أمكن ويبتاع بما بقي ما ينتفع بعينه، ذکره صاحب الهداية. وفي الكافي : الأصح أنه لا يجب ذلك وليس للأب أن يفعله من مال الصغير.
Thus it passed concerning meaning that it is not obligatory on him. And his saying 'for himself' because he is the origin of obligation. And his saying 'not for his child' concerning his young children because it is a pure act of worship unlike sadaqat al-fitr. And the first is the apparent narration. And if the minor has wealth, his father sacrifices from his own wealth or from the minor's wealth according to Abu Hanifa. And Muhammad, Zafar, and al-Shafi'i said: from the father's wealth, because the bloodshed is destruction and the father does not have ownership over the minor's wealth like manumission. And the most correct is that he sacrifices from his own wealth and eats from it as much as possible and buys with the remainder what benefits in its essence, mentioned by the author of Al-Hidayah. And in Al-Kafi: the most correct is that it is not obligatory and the father should not do it from the minor's wealth.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage addresses whether udhiyah is obligatory for oneself and whether a father may sacrifice from his minor child's wealth. The matn states 'for himself' indicating the obligation is on the adult. For children, it is a pure act of worship, unlike sadaqat al-fitr. The apparent narration is that it is not obligatory for children. Then there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that if the child has wealth, the father may sacrifice from the child's wealth. Muhammad, Zafar, and al-Shafi'i say it must be from the father's wealth, reasoning that slaughter is destruction (itlaf) and the father does not own the child's property, analogous to manumission. The most correct view according to Al-Hidayah is that the father should sacrifice from his own wealth and may eat from it. Al-Kafi states it is not obligatory and the father should not use the child's wealth. This shows the spectrum of opinions within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted rulings from the core books of the Hanafi school (the six books of Imam Muhammad).
القربة المحضة (al-qurbah al-mahdah): A pure act of worship with no worldly benefit, unlike sadaqat al-fitr which also has a social welfare aspect.
الإراقة (al-iraqah): The spilling of blood (slaughter) of the sacrifice animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zafar, from whose wealth should the father sacrifice for his minor child?
A) From the father's own wealth.
B) From the child's wealth if the child has money.
C) From both, if possible.
D) Sacrifice for the child is not required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether udhiyah for a minor can be performed from the minor's wealth; the relied-upon position is that it must be from the father's own wealth.
