Joint Sacrifice: One Cow or Camel for Seven Persons
Explains permissibility of sharing cow/camel among seven, with hadith and qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory quantity for udhiyah in the Hanafi school and explain the role of qiyas and istihsan in permitting joint sacrifice.

Core Idea
One cow or camel may be shared by seven persons — a hadith overrides strict analogy in Hanafi law.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the obligatory amount for udhiyah as stated in the matn. The author demonstrates how a hadith from Jabir (ra) serves as an exception to the default qiyas that each animal should be for one person only. It also addresses practical scenarios like sharing the animal and joining partners after purchase, illustrating the Hanafi use of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligatory quantity from the matn
The matn states the wajib as either a sheep or a seventh of a camel/cow. Recognize that 'shah' is one sheep, and 'sub' badana' is one-seventh of a cow or camel. This is the minimum obligation per person.
Sheep cannot be shared; it is for one person only.
2
Understand the default qiyas (analogy)
By strict analogy, a whole animal should be for one person because slaughter is an indivisible act of worship (qurbah). Without a specific text, sharing would be invalid.
Qiyas yields: one animal = one person.
3
Examine the overriding dalil (athar)
The hadith from Jabir (ra) explicitly permits a cow or camel for seven persons. This specific transmitted text (athar) takes priority over qiyas, illustrating the Hanafi principle that a narrated exception overrides general analogy.
Hadith: 'Naharna ma'a Rasul Allah al-baqara 'an sab'ah wa al-badana 'an sab'ah'.
4
Apply the ruling to other numbers and conditions
Since sharing for seven is allowed, less than seven (e.g., six, five, four, three, two) is also allowed, and the meat must be divided by weight, not guesswork. However, eight is not allowed due to lack of textual support.
If one partner's share is less than one-seventh, the sacrifice is invalid for all.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on joining partners after purchase
The text presents a conflict: if someone buys an animal intending sacrifice and later finds partners, does it suffice? By strict qiyas (Zafar and a narration from Abu Hanifa), no, because the animal becomes dedicated. But by istihsan (the relied-upon view), yes, due to practical need.
The preferred view is istihsan, but better practice is to arrange partners before purchase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله «شاة أو سبع بدنة بيان للقدر الواجب والقياس أن لا يجوز إلا البدنة كلها إلا عن واحد لأن الإراقة قربة لا تتجزي إلا أنا تركناه بالأثر وهو ما روي عن جابر رضي الله تعالى عنه قال : نحرنا مع رسول الله البقرة عن سبعة والبدنة عن سبعة ولا نص في الشاة فبقي على أصل القياس وتجوز عن ستة أو خمسة أو أربعة أو ثلاثة، ذكره في الأصل لأنه لما جاز عن سبعة فما دونها أولى ولا يجوز عن ثمانية لعدم النقل فيه، وكذا إذا كان نصيب أحدهم أقل من سبع بدنة لا يجوز عن الكل لأن بعضه إذا خرج عن كونه قربة كله، ويجوز عن اثنين نصفاً في الأصح ، وإذا جاز عن الشركة يقسم اللحم بالوزن لأنه موزون. وإذا قسموا جزافاً لا يجوز إلا إذا كان معه شيء آخر من الأكارع والجلد كالبيع لأن القسمة فيها معنى المبادلة ولو اشترى بقرة يريد أن يضحي ثم اشترك فيها معه ستة أجزأه استحساناً، والقياس لا يجزئ وهو قول زفر لأنه أعدها قربة فيمتنع بيعها. وجه الاستحسان أنه قد يجد بقرة سمينة وقد لا يظفر بالشركاء وقت الشراء فيشتريها ثم يطلب الشركاء، ولو لم يجز ذلك لخرجوا وهو مدفوع شرعاً، والأحسن أن يفعل ذلك قبل الشراء، وعن الإمام مثل قوله زفر.
And his statement 'a sheep or a seventh of a camel' is an explanation of the obligatory amount. By analogy (qiyas), it is not permissible except a whole camel for one person, because the bloodshed is an act of worship that does not split. But we abandoned the analogy based on the athar, which is narrated from Jabir (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: We slaughtered a cow for seven and a camel for seven with the Messenger of Allah. There is no text regarding a sheep, so it remains on the original analogy. And it is permissible for six, five, four, or three, as mentioned in Al-Asl, because if it is permissible for seven, then less is more appropriate. It is not permissible for eight due to lack of transmission. Similarly, if the share of one of them is less than a seventh of a camel, it is not permissible for all because if part of it leaves the state of being a worship, the whole does. It is permissible for two by half according to the most correct. And if sharing is allowed, the meat is divided by weight because it is weighable. If they divide by guess (jizaf), it is not permissible unless there is something else with it like the trotters and skin, similar to a sale, because division has the meaning of exchange. If someone bought a cow intending to sacrifice, then six others joined him in it, it suffices by istihsan. By qiyas it would not suffice, which is the view of Zafar, because he dedicated it for worship, so its sale is prohibited. The basis of istihsan is that one may find a fat cow but may not find partners at the time of purchase, so he buys it and then seeks partners; if it were not allowed, they would be deprived, which is repelled by the Shari'ah. The better practice is to do that before purchase. And from the Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is reported like the view of Zafar.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explains the obligatory quantity for udhiyah as stated in the matn: a sheep for one person or one-seventh of a camel/cow. By strict analogy (qiyas), a camel should be for only one person, because slaughter is an indivisible act of worship. However, the Hanafis depart from this based on a hadith from Jabir that the Prophet allowed a cow and a camel for seven persons. This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) overriding qiyas. For sheep, there is no such narration, so the original qiyas remains: one sheep per person. Sharing among fewer than seven is also allowed. Meat distribution among partners must be by weight, not by guess, because meat is a weighable commodity and guesswork resembles a sale without proper consideration. The passage also discusses a scenario where someone buys a cow intending sacrifice and then later finds partners. By istihsan, this is valid, although Zafar and a report from Abu Hanifa reject it, following qiyas that once dedicated, the animal cannot be sold (since it becomes a qurbah). The preferred practice is to secure partners before purchase.

Key Terms
شاة أو سبع بدنة (shaatun aw sub'u badana): The obligatory minimum: one sheep or one-seventh of a camel/cow.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, the original analogy would require one animal per person.
الأثر (al-athar): A transmitted report, here the hadith of Jabir.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that abandons strict analogy for a more lenient ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith from Jabir, for how many persons can a cow be sacrificed?
A) Seven
B) Two
C) Ten
D) Only one

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a cow or camel may be sacrificed on behalf of up to seven persons, overriding the default qiyas by the explicit hadith from Jabir (ra).

Ranking of Obligations in Hanafi Fiqh
States ranking of three wajibs by emphasis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relative emphasis of sajdat al-tilawah, sadaqat al-fitr, and udhiyah, and explain that all are wajib yet differ in strength.

Core Idea
Udhiyah is wajib but less emphasized than sadaqat al-fitr and sajdat al-tilawah.

Not all wajibs are equal in emphasis. Imam al-Quduri establishes a hierarchy among three obligations: sujood al-tilawah, sadaqat al-fitr, and udhiyah. Understanding this ranking helps prioritize when resources are limited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three obligations
Read the first sentence: 'Obligations are on levels, some more emphasized than others.' Then list: sajdat al-tilawah, sadaqat al-fitr, udhiyah.
All three are wajib, not sunnah.
2
Order them by emphasis
The text states: sajdat al-tilawah > sadaqat al-fitr > udhiyah. Understand that 'more emphasized' means stronger obligation.
This ranking is specific to Hanafi school.
3
Recognize practical implication
If one cannot do all, the more emphasized wajib takes precedence. For example, if a person has only enough time or money for one, they should prioritize sajdat al-tilawah (if needed) over sadaqat al-fitr, etc.
This is a principle of taqdim al-ahamm (prioritizing the most important).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القدوري: الواجب على مراتب بعضها أكد من بعض، ووجوب سجدة التلاوة أكد من وجوب صدقة الفطر، وصدقة الفطر وجوبها آكد من وجوب الأضحية. وفي الخانية : الموسر في ظاهر الرواية من له مائتا درهم أو عشرون ديناراً أو ما بلغ ذلك سوى سكنه ومتاعه ومركبه وخادمه الذي في حاجته وفي الأصل ولو جاء يوم الأضحية ولا استفاد مائتي درهم ولا دين عليه فعليه الأضحية ولو كان له عقار ملك قيمة العقار مائة درهم، والزعفراني والفقيه علي الرازي اعتبر القيمة وأوجبا الأضحية. ولو كان له أرض يدخل عليه منها قوت السنة فعليه الأضحية حيث كان القوت يكفيه ويكفي عياله، وإن كان لا يكفيه فهو معسر.
Al-Quduri said: Obligations are on levels, some more emphasized than others. The obligation of sajdat al-tilawah is more emphasized than the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr, and sadaqat al-fitr is more emphasized than the obligation of udhiyah. And in Al-Khaniyah: The wealthy person according to the apparent narration is one who has two hundred dirhams or twenty dinars or their equivalent, aside from his dwelling, household goods, mount, and servant he needs. In Al-Asl: If the day of sacrifice comes and he has not acquired two hundred dirhams and has no debt, then udhiyah is obligatory on him. If he owns a property whose value is one hundred dirhams, Al-Za'farani and Al-Faqih Ali al-Razi considered the value and made udhiyah obligatory. If he has land from which he gets his yearly provision, then udhiyah is obligatory if the provision suffices him and his family; if it does not suffice, he is poor (mu'sir).
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage starts with a famous statement from Imam al-Quduri ranking different wajibs: sujood al-tilawah is most emphasized, then sadaqat al-fitr, then udhiyah. This indicates that while udhiyah is obligatory, it is the least emphasized. Then it defines the person upon whom udhiyah is obligatory (musir). According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), a musir is someone who possesses 200 dirhams or 20 dinars or their equivalent, over and above essential needs: house, furniture, mount, and servant. If one has a property worth 100 dirhams, some scholars like Al-Za'farani and Ali al-Razi consider the value and obligate udhiyah. For a person with land providing yearly sustenance, if it suffices for him and his family, udhiyah is due; otherwise he is considered poor (mu'sir) and not obligated.

Key Terms
المراتب (al-maratib): Levels or ranks of obligations according to emphasis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Quduri, which obligation is most emphasized?
A) Udhiyah
B) Sadaqat al-Fitr
C) Sajdat al-Tilawah
D) They are all equally emphasized

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, not all wajibs carry equal weight; knowing the hierarchy helps in proper prioritization.

Definition of Musir for Udhiyah
Defines the wealth threshold for udhiyah obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the minimum wealth threshold (200 dirhams or 20 dinars above needs) for the obligation of udhiyah and apply it to various asset scenarios.

Core Idea
Udhiyah is wajib only on a musir who has 200 dirhams or 20 dinars beyond essentials like home, furniture, mount, and servant.

After establishing the ranking of obligations, the passage focuses on udhiyah and defines who is obligated. The Hanafi school sets a clear monetary threshold based on apparent narrations, but there is an intra-school dispute about considering property value. Understanding this definition is crucial for determining when udhiyah is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stated threshold
From Al-Khaniyah: musir is one who has 200 dirhams or 20 dinars or equivalent, excluding dwelling, household goods, mount, and servant.
These exclusions are basic necessities of life.
2
Analyze the property case
If one has property worth 100 dirhams, Al-Za'farani and Ali al-Razi consider the value and obligate udhiyah. This shows an intra-Hanafi difference.
The majority (zahir al-riwaya) does not count property as part of the nisab.
3
Understand the land provision case
If one has land providing yearly sustenance, examine whether it suffices for him and his family. If yes, udhiyah is due; if not, he is poor (mu'sir) and exempt.
This indicates that the threshold is about surplus beyond ongoing living expenses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القدوري: الواجب على مراتب بعضها أكد من بعض، ووجوب سجدة التلاوة أكد من وجوب صدقة الفطر، وصدقة الفطر وجوبها آكد من وجوب الأضحية. وفي الخانية : الموسر في ظاهر الرواية من له مائتا درهم أو عشرون ديناراً أو ما بلغ ذلك سوى سكنه ومتاعه ومركبه وخادمه الذي في حاجته وفي الأصل ولو جاء يوم الأضحية ولا استفاد مائتي درهم ولا دين عليه فعليه الأضحية ولو كان له عقار ملك قيمة العقار مائة درهم، والزعفراني والفقيه علي الرازي اعتبر القيمة وأوجبا الأضحية. ولو كان له أرض يدخل عليه منها قوت السنة فعليه الأضحية حيث كان القوت يكفيه ويكفي عياله، وإن كان لا يكفيه فهو معسر.
Al-Quduri said: Obligations are on levels, some more emphasized than others. The obligation of sajdat al-tilawah is more emphasized than the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr, and sadaqat al-fitr is more emphasized than the obligation of udhiyah. And in Al-Khaniyah: The wealthy person according to the apparent narration is one who has two hundred dirhams or twenty dinars or their equivalent, aside from his dwelling, household goods, mount, and servant he needs. In Al-Asl: If the day of sacrifice comes and he has not acquired two hundred dirhams and has no debt, then udhiyah is obligatory on him. If he owns a property whose value is one hundred dirhams, Al-Za'farani and Al-Faqih Ali al-Razi considered the value and made udhiyah obligatory. If he has land from which he gets his yearly provision, then udhiyah is obligatory if the provision suffices him and his family; if it does not suffice, he is poor (mu'sir).
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage starts with a famous statement from Imam al-Quduri ranking different wajibs: sujood al-tilawah is most emphasized, then sadaqat al-fitr, then udhiyah. This indicates that while udhiyah is obligatory, it is the least emphasized. Then it defines the person upon whom udhiyah is obligatory (musir). According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), a musir is someone who possesses 200 dirhams or 20 dinars or their equivalent, over and above essential needs: house, furniture, mount, and servant. If one has a property worth 100 dirhams, some scholars like Al-Za'farani and Ali al-Razi consider the value and obligate udhiyah. For a person with land providing yearly sustenance, if it suffices for him and his family, udhiyah is due; otherwise he is considered poor (mu'sir) and not obligated.

Key Terms
الموسر (al-musir): The wealthy person who is obligated to perform udhiyah.
المعسر (al-mu'sir): One who is unable to afford udhiyah, i.e., poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any amount of savings
B) 200 dirhams or 20 dinars above essential needs
C) 100 dirhams
D) 300 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a precise definition of wealth for udhiyah: 200 dirhams (or 20 dinars) above basic living needs, with some internal debate about property.

6 min
Waqf Property and Udhiyah Obligation
Waqf yield assessment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state how waqf property yield is considered for the obligation of udhiyah.

Core Idea
Only the actual yield from waqf during sacrifice days counts toward wealth.

This lesson addresses a specific case: a person who benefits from a waqf (endowment) during the days of sacrifice. The ruling determines whether the yield from the waqf makes udhiyah obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
The person does not own the waqf capital; he only receives its produce.
Waqf is not owned by the beneficiary.
2
Determine the relevant period
Only yield obtained during the days of sacrifice (10th–12th Dhul Hijjah) counts.
3
Apply the wealth threshold
If the yield equals or exceeds 200 dirhams, udhiyah is wajib; otherwise not.
4
Note the conflicting narration
Ibn Sama'ah narrated a view that if the yield exceeds 200, it is not obligatory.
This is a weaker narration; the first view is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان العقار وقفاً ينظر؛ إن وجب له في أيام النحر قدر مائتي درهم فعليه الأضحية وإلا فلا. رواه ابن سماعة عن محمد عن الإمام وعنه أنه لا يجب إذا زاد على مائتين. والمرأة تعتبر موسرة بالمهر إذا الزوج ملياً عندهما، وعند الإمام لا تعتبر ملية بذلك. وإن كان خباز عنده حنطة قيمتها مائتا درهم فعليه الأضحية وإن كان عنده مصحف قيمته درهم وهو ممن يحسن القراءة فيه فلا أضحية عليه سواء كان يقرأ فيه أو لا يقرأ فيه، وإن كان لا يحسن أن لا يقرأ فيه فعليه الأضحية. وفي الكافي عن الحسن عن الإمام يجب عليه أن يضحي عن ولده وولد ولده الذي لا أب له ؛ والفتوى على أنه لا يجب عليه. وذكر الصدر الشهيد في شرح الأضاحي عن الزعفراني فميا إذا ضحى الأب عن الصغير من فعلى قول محمد وزفر يجب الضمان عليه وعلى قول الإمام أبي يوسف لا يضمن ومثله الوصي. وفي الينابيع والمعتوه والمجنون بمنزلة الصبي والذي يجن ويفيق كالصحيح، ولو كان المجنون موسراً يضحي عنه وليه من ماله في الروايات المشهورة، وروي أن الأضحية قبل يضحي بها لا تجب في مال المجنون.
And if the property is a waqf, it is considered: if he is entitled during the days of sacrifice to the amount of two hundred dirhams, then udhiyah is obligatory on him; otherwise not. Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Muhammad from the Imam, and from him that it is not obligatory if it exceeds two hundred. And a woman is considered wealthy with the dower if the husband is wealthy according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), she is not considered wealthy by that. And if a baker has wheat worth two hundred dirhams, udhiyah is obligatory on him. And if he has a Quran worth one dirham and he is one who can read well from it, then there is no udhiyah on him, whether he reads from it or not. And if he cannot read from it well, then udhiyah is obligatory. In Al-Kafi from Al-Hasan from the Imam: he must sacrifice for his child and his grandchild who has no father; but the fatwa is that it is not obligatory. And Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Sharh al-Adahi from Al-Za'farani regarding if the father sacrifices for the minor: according to Muhammad and Zafar, compensation (daman) is required; according to Imam Abu Yusuf, no compensation, and similarly for the guardian (wasi). In Al-Yanabi': the mentally deficient (ma'tuh) and insane (majnun) are like the minor; one who becomes insane intermittently is like the healthy. If the insane person is wealthy, his guardian sacrifices from his wealth according to the well-known narrations; and it is narrated that udhiyah before sacrificing is not obligatory on the insane person's wealth.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage provides specific rulings on wealth and obligation. For waqf property, only the actual yield during the days of sacrifice counts. A conflicting narration says if the yield exceeds 200, it is not obligatory (maybe because the owner does not have full ownership). A woman is considered wealthy if she has a dower due from a wealthy husband according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; Abu Hanifa differs. A baker with wheat worth 200 dirhams must sacrifice. Regarding a Quran worth 1 dirham: if the owner can read well, it is considered a personal utility item and does not count toward wealth; if he cannot read, it is a commodity. Regarding children: a narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa says sacrifice for a child without a father is obligatory, but the fatwa is that it is not. There is a dispute on compensation if a father sacrifices for a minor: Muhammad and Zafar say he must guarantee, Abu Yusuf says no. For the insane and mentally deficient, they are like minors; if wealthy, their guardian sacrifices from their wealth according to the famous narrations, but a contrary narration says it is not obligatory.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowed property; only the yield during sacrifice days counts for wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view, what triggers udhiyah obligation for a person with waqf property?
A) The capital value of the waqf reaching 200 dirhams.
B) The yield from the waqf during the sacrifice days reaching 200 dirhams.
C) Owning any waqf property.
D) The yield exceeding 200 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
Only the yield from waqf during the days of sacrifice counts toward the wealth threshold for udhiyah.

Baker's Wheat as Trade Goods for Udhiyah
Trade goods as wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that trade goods (like a baker's wheat) count as wealth for udhiyah obligation.

Core Idea
A baker's wheat stock valued at 200 dirhams makes udhiyah obligatory.

This lesson covers the case of a baker who possesses wheat as part of his trade. The ruling clarifies that such stock is considered wealth, and if it reaches the threshold, udhiyah is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asset
The baker has wheat that is inventory for his business.
This is an asset, not a personal necessity.
2
Assess its value
If the wheat's market value is 200 dirhams or more, it triggers obligation.
Value is determined at the time of the sacrifice days.
3
Determine the ruling
The text explicitly says: 'فعليه الأضحية' – udhiyah is obligatory.

Quran as Personal Necessity and Udhiyah
Personal necessity exclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a Quran copy in wealth calculation for udhiyah.

Core Idea
A Quran worth 1 dirham is excluded from wealth if the owner can read from it.

This lesson addresses whether owning a copy of the Quran makes one wealthy for the purpose of udhiyah. The ruling distinguishes based on whether the owner can read from it: if he can, it is a personal necessity and excluded; if he cannot, it is a commodity and counts toward wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item
A person possesses a Quran copy worth 1 dirham.
The value is minimal but still an asset.
2
Assess the owner's ability to read
If the owner can read the Quran well, it is considered a personal necessity.
If he cannot read, it is not a personal need.
3
Apply the two sub-rulings
If he can read: no udhiyah. If he cannot read: udhiyah is wajib.
Whether he actually reads it or not is irrelevant; ability matters.

Woman's Dower (Mahr) as Wealth for Udhiyah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on whether a woman's receivable dower counts as wealth for udhiyah.

Core Idea
A woman is considered wealthy by her dower if the husband is wealthy, according to the Sahibayn.

This lesson covers the situation of a woman who has a dower (mahr) owed to her. The ruling depends on whether the husband is wealthy and on the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asset
The woman is entitled to a dower from her husband.
This is a debt receivable.
2
Consider the husband's financial status
If the husband is wealthy (mali'), the dower is considered due and collectible.
3
Apply the two positions
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, she is considered wealthy by that dower. According to Abu Hanifa, she is not considered wealthy because it is only a receivable.
The text says 'عندهما' (according to the two) and 'عند الإمام لا تعتبر'.

Source Text

Sacrifice for the Child and Compensation
Child's sacrifice and compensation dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sacrifice for a child without a father and the dispute on compensation.

Core Idea
The fatwa: a father is not obligated to sacrifice for his child; if he does, no compensation due according to Abu Yusuf.

This lesson addresses two issues: whether a father must sacrifice for his minor child (orphan of father) and if he does sacrifice, whether he must compensate the child's estate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation issue
Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the father must sacrifice for his child who has no father (i.e., the child is a paternal orphan). However, the fatwa is that it is not obligatory.
The fatwa overrides the narration.
2
Identify the compensation issue
If the father does sacrifice for the minor, there is a dispute: Muhammad and Zafar say he must guarantee (daman); Abu Yusuf says no.
The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view.
3
Extend the compensation ruling
The same applies to a guardian (wasi) who sacrifices for the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if the property is a waqf, it is considered: if he is entitled during the days of sacrifice to the amount of two hundred dirhams, then udhiyah is obligatory on him; otherwise not. Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Muhammad from the Imam, and from him that it is not obligatory if it exceeds two hundred. And a woman is considered wealthy with the dower if the husband is wealthy according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), she is not considered wealthy by that. And if a baker has wheat worth two hundred dirhams, udhiyah is obligatory on him. And if he has a Quran worth one dirham and he is one who can read well from it, then there is no udhiyah on him, whether he reads from it or not. And if he cannot read from it well, then udhiyah is obligatory. In Al-Kafi from Al-Hasan from the Imam: he must sacrifice for his child and his grandchild who has no father; but the fatwa is that it is not obligatory. And Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Sharh al-Adahi from Al-Za'farani regarding if the father sacrifices for the minor: according to Muhammad and Zafar, compensation (daman) is required; according to Imam Abu Yusuf, no compensation, and similarly for the guardian (wasi). In Al-Yanabi': the mentally deficient (ma'tuh) and insane (majnun) are like the minor; one who becomes insane intermittently is like the healthy. If the insane person is wealthy, his guardian sacrifices from his wealth according to the well-known narrations; and it is narrated that udhiyah before sacrificing is not obligatory on the insane person's wealth.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage provides specific rulings on wealth and obligation. For waqf property, only the actual yield during the days of sacrifice counts. A conflicting narration says if the yield exceeds 200, it is not obligatory (maybe because the owner does not have full ownership). A woman is considered wealthy if she has a dower due from a wealthy husband according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; Abu Hanifa differs. A baker with wheat worth 200 dirhams must sacrifice. Regarding a Quran worth 1 dirham: if the owner can read well, it is considered a personal utility item and does not count toward wealth; if he cannot read, it is a commodity. Regarding children: a narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa says sacrifice for a child without a father is obligatory, but the fatwa is that it is not. There is a dispute on compensation if a father sacrifices for a minor: Muhammad and Zafar say he must guarantee, Abu Yusuf says no. For the insane and mentally deficient, they are like minors; if wealthy, their guardian sacrifices from their wealth according to the famous narrations, but a contrary narration says it is not obligatory.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Compensation; in this context, financial liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa, what is the ruling on a father sacrificing for his minor child?
A) It is obligatory.
B) It is not obligatory, and no compensation required.
C) It is not allowed.
D) It is obligatory and compensation is required.

Key Takeaway
The father is not obligated to sacrifice for his child, and if he does, no compensation is due per the fatwa.

Rulings for the Insane and Mentally Deficient
Insane person's sacrifice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on udhiyah for an insane or ma'tuh person.

Core Idea
An insane person is treated like a minor; if wealthy, his guardian sacrifices from his wealth.

This lesson covers the ruling for individuals who are insane (majnun) or mentally deficient (ma'tuh) regarding udhiyah. They are treated analogously to minors, and their guardian is responsible to sacrifice on their behalf if they are wealthy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the person's condition
The person is either always insane, intermittently insane, or mentally deficient (ma'tuh).
2
Analogize to the minor
The text says: 'بمنزلة الصبي' – they are like the minor.
Thus rulings for minors apply.
3
Note the exception for intermittent insanity
One who becomes insane intermittently is treated as sane (like a sane adult).
Because he is sane most of the time.
4
Determine the guardian's duty
If the insane person is wealthy, his guardian must sacrifice from his wealth, according to the famous narrations.
A contrary narration says it is not obligatory, but the famous view is acted upon.

How the Obligation of Udhiyah Arises: Wealth, Intent, and Verbalization
A collection of rulings on udhiyah animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish how udhiyah becomes obligatory for a rich vs poor person and determine the rulings when a sacrificial animal is lost, stolen, or substituted.

Core Idea
Udhiyah obligation depends on wealth; the poor are bound by purchase with intention, the rich only by verbal declaration.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq presents a series of scenarios on the purchase, loss, and substitution of udhiyah animals. It highlights a key distinction: the way obligation arises differs between a wealthy person (ghani) and a poor person (faqir). Within the Hanafi school, there are significant internal disputes on whether mere purchase with intention obligates the poor, and how verbalization affects the ruling. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the rules of sacrifice in various everyday situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the financial status
The first step in any udhiyah scenario is to determine whether the person is rich (ghani) or poor (faqir). This classification governs how obligation arises.
The text considers a person absent from his wealth during the days of sacrifice as poor.
2
Determine how obligation arises
For the rich, udhiyah becomes obligatory only by verbal declaration (e.g., saying 'I make this my udhiyah'). For the poor, there is a dispute: according to Khawahirzada, mere purchase with intention makes it wajib; according to al-Za'farani, it does not.
The preferred view (supported by Sarakhsi and Halwani) is that purchase with intention obligates the poor.
3
Analyze loss and substitution scenarios
If the animal dies or is lost before slaughter, the ruling depends on substitution. If a poor person's sheep is stolen and he buys another, then finds the first, he must sacrifice both. If it was merely lost, he need not replace it unless rich.
For a rich person, loss obligates replacement regardless.
4
Apply the verbalization condition in substitutions
If a person loses his declared sacrifice and buys a second one, then finds the first, the outcome depends on whether he verbalized the second as a replacement or as a new obligation. If he said 'this is a substitute', he may choose which to slaughter; if he declared it anew, both must be sacrificed.
Abu Yusuf holds that mere intention does not create a sacrifice – verbalization is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى اشترى شاة ليضحي بها فمات في أيام الأضحية قبل أن يضحي بها فله أن يبيعها، ومن كان غائباً عن ماله في أيام الأضحية فهو فقير، ولا يخفى أن الأضحية تصير واجبة بالنذر فلو قال كلاماً نفسياً الله علي أن أضحي بهذه الشاة ولم يذكر بلسانه شيئاً فاشترى شاة بنية الأضحية إن كان المشتري غنياً لا تصير واجبة باتفاق الروايات فله أن يبيعها ويشتري غيرها، وإن كان فقيراً ذكر شيخ الإسلام خواهر زاده في ظاهر الرواية تصير واجبة بنفس الشراء، وروى الزعفراني عن أصحابنا لا تصير واجبة. وأشار إليه شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه وإليه مال شمس الأئمة الحلواني في شرحه وقال : إنه ظاهر الرواية ولو صرح بلسانه والمسألة بحالها تصير واجبة بشراء نية الأضحية إن كان المشتري فقيراً. وفي الخانية : اشترى شاة للأضحية ثم باعها واشترى أخرى في أيام النحر فهذا على وجوه ثلاثة : الأول اشترى شاة ينوي بها الأضحية لا تصير أضحية ما لم يوجبها بلسانه وبه أخذ أبو يوسف وبعض المتأخرين. وفي الكبرى : قال إن فعلت كذا علي أن أضحي لا يكون يميناً رجل اشترى أضحيته وأوجبها فضلت ثم اشترى أخرى فأوجبها ثم وجد الأولى؛ إن كان أوجب الثانية بلسانه فعليه أن يضحي بهما، وإن أوجبها بدلاً. عن الأولى فعليه أن يذبح أيهما شاء ولم يفصل بين الفقير والغني، وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند : الفقير إذا أوجب شاة على نفسه هل يحل له أن يأكل منها ؟ قال بديع الدين : نعم . وقال القاضي برهان الدين لا يحل وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند الفقير إذا اشترى شاة للأضحية فسرقت فاشترى مكانها ثم وجد الأولى فعليه أن يضحي بهما، ولو ضلت فليس عليه أن يشتري أخرى مكانها وإن كان غنياً فعليه أن يشتري أخرى مكانها. وفي الواقعات : له مائتا درهم فاشترى بعشرين درهماً أضحية يوم الثلاث وهلكت يوم الأربعاء وجاء يوم
In Al-Muntaqa: If one bought a sheep to sacrifice with it and it died during the days of sacrifice before he sacrificed, he may sell it. And one who is absent from his wealth during the days of sacrifice is poor. It is well known that udhiyah becomes obligatory by vow (nadhr). So if one said to himself 'upon me is to sacrifice this sheep' without uttering it with his tongue, then he bought a sheep with the intention of udhiyah: if the buyer is rich, it does not become obligatory by agreement of narrations, and he may sell it and buy another. If he is poor, Shaykh al-Islam Khawahirzada mentioned that in the apparent narration, it becomes obligatory by the purchase itself; Al-Za'farani narrated from our companions that it does not become obligatory. Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi alluded to it in his commentary and Shams al-Aimmah al-Halwani inclined to it, saying it is the apparent narration: even if he did not verbalize, it becomes obligatory by purchase if the buyer is poor. In Al-Khaniyah: If one bought a sheep for udhiyah then sold it and bought another during the days of sacrifice, this has three aspects: first, buying a sheep intending udhiyah does not make it a sacrifice unless he verbalizes the obligation; this is the view of Abu Yusuf and some later scholars. In Al-Kubra: If one said 'if I do such-and-such, upon me is to sacrifice' it is not an oath. A man bought his sacrifice and declared it obligatory, then it was lost, so he bought another and declared it obligatory, then found the first; if he declared the second by utterance, he must sacrifice both; if he declared it as a replacement for the first, he may slaughter whichever he wishes, without distinguishing between rich and poor. In the Fatawa of the people of Samarkand: If a poor person declares a sheep obligatory on himself, is it permissible for him to eat from it? Badr al-Din said yes; Qadi Burhan al-Din said no. In the same Fatawa: If a poor person bought a sheep for udhiyah and it was stolen, then he bought another in its place, then found the first, he must sacrifice both. If it was lost (not stolen), he does not have to buy another; but if he is rich, he must buy another. In Al-Waqi'at: One who has 200 dirhams bought a sacrifice for 20 dirhams on Tuesday, and it perished on Wednesday, and then came the day (of sacrifice)...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage collects numerous rulings on the purchase, loss, and substitution of udhiyah animals. Key points include: if a sheep dies before slaughter, it can be sold. A person absent from his wealth is considered poor. Udhiyah becomes obligatory by vow. For a poor person, there is a dispute: according to Khawahirzada, purchase with intention makes it obligatory; Al-Za'farani disagrees. The view of Sarakhsi and Halwani supports Khawahirzada. For a rich person, verbal declaration is required. In Al-Khaniyah, buying with intention alone does not make it a sacrifice; verbalization is needed (view of Abu Yusuf). If one loses his sacrifice, buys another, then finds the first, the ruling depends on whether he verbalized the second as a replacement. The Fatawa of Samarkand address whether a poor person can eat from his own sacrifice; two opinions. Also, if a sheep is stolen, he must sacrifice both if he finds the first later; if lost, no need to replace unless rich. The passage ends with an incomplete case of a person with 200 dirhams buying a sheep that perishes.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow that makes udhiyah obligatory.
الغني (al-ghani): A wealthy person; for him, udhiyah becomes obligatory only by verbal declaration.
الفقير (al-faqir): A poor person; for him, purchase with intention may make udhiyah obligatory.
الضمان (al-daman): Compensation or guarantee, e.g., if father sacrifices from child's wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (mufta bihi), when does a sacrifice become obligatory for a poor person?
A) Only after verbal declaration
B) By the mere purchase with intention
C) Only after slaughter
D) Only if the animal is lost

Key Takeaway
The obligation of udhiyah hinges on wealth and expression: the poor are bound by intention-purchase, the rich only by verbal declaration, and loss/substitution rules differ accordingly.

Defining Wealth for Udhiyah: Nisab, Income, and Tools
Rulings on what constitutes wealth for udhiyah obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria for wealth (nisab of 200 dirhams) to determine whether udhiyah is obligatory, including cases of waqf income, trade inventory, and personal tools.

Core Idea
Udhiyah is wajib on the wealthy; wealth is measured by nisab after debts and necessities.

Udhiyah (sacrifice) is an obligation upon every financially able Muslim. But what exactly counts as 'wealth'? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi criteria: the nisab threshold of 200 dirhams, how income from waqf is assessed, whether inventory (like a baker's wheat) makes one wealthy, and why a Quran valued at one dirham may exempt you if it is your tool of worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Udhiyah is wajib (obligatory) on every Muslim who meets the wealth condition. First, confirm the hukm level: Hanafi school holds it wajib, not sunnah.
The Shafi'i school differs, considering it sunnah mu'akkadah.
2
Define the wealthy (mūsir) and insolvent (mu'sir)
The text defines mu'sir as one whose wealth is insufficient. Mūsir is one who owns nisab after basic needs. The measure is 200 dirhams (or equivalent) available during the days of slaughter.
Basic needs include housing, food, clothing, and tools of trade.
3
Apply nisab to specific assets
For waqf property, it's the income received during the days of slaughter that counts, not the capital value. For a baker, wheat inventory worth 200 dirhams makes him wealthy. For a Quran worth a dirham, it only counts if he cannot read it; if he can, it is a necessary tool and excluded.
The rule: assets used as tools of trade or worship are not counted toward nisab.
4
Consider cumulative wealth over years
A narrated view from Imam Muhammad (via Ibn Sama'ah) states that if one's wealth exceeds 200 dirhams in a previous year but is now less, udhiyah is not obligatory. Nisab is assessed annually on the days of slaughter.
This is similar to zakat but assessed at a specific time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن كتاب الأضحية ماله لا يكفيه فهو معسر. وإن كان العقار وقفاً ينظر؛ إن وجب له في أيام النحر قدر مائتي درهم فعليه الأضحية وإلا فلا. رواه ابن سماعة عن محمد عن الإمام وعنه أنه لا يجب إذا زاد على مائتين. والمرأة تعتبر موسرة بالمهر إذا الزوج ملياً عندهما، وعند الإمام لا تعتبر ملية بذلك. وإن كان خباز عنده حنطة قيمتها مائتا درهم فعليه الأضحية وإن كان عنده مصحف قيمته درهم وهو ممن يحسن القراءة فيه فلا أضحية عليه سواء كان يقرأ فيه أو لا يقرأ فيه، وإن كان لا يحسن أن لا يقرأ فيه فعليه الأضحية. وفي الكافي عن الحسن عن الإمام يجب عليه أن يضحي عن ولده وولد ولده الذي لا أب له؛ والفتوى على أنه لا يجب عليه. وذكر الصدر الشهيد في شرح الأضاحي عن الزعفراني فيما إذا ضحى الأب عن الصغير من فعلى قول محمد وزفر يجب الضمان عليه وعلى قول الإمام أبي يوسف لا يضمن ومثله الوصي. وفي الينابيع والمعتوه والمجنون بمنزلة الصبي والذي يجن ويفيق كالصحيح، ولو كان المجنون موسراً يضحي عنه وليه من ماله في الروايات المشهورة، وروي أن الأضحية قبل يضحي بها لا تجب في مال المجنون.
That the book of Udhiyah: he whose wealth does not suffice him is insolvent. And if the real estate is a waqf, it is considered: if he is entitled to receive during the days of slaughter the amount of two hundred dirhams, then the udhiyah is obligatory upon him, otherwise not. It was narrated by Ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad from the Imam, and from him that it is not obligatory if it exceeds two hundred. And the woman is considered wealthy by the dowry if the husband is solvent according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to the Imam she is not considered wealthy by that. And if a baker has wheat worth two hundred dirhams, then the udhiyah is upon him. And if he has a mushaf worth a dirham while he is one who can read it well, then there is no udhiyah upon him, whether he reads from it or does not. And if he is not skilled in it, then upon him is the udhiyah. And in al-Kafi from al-Hasan from the Imam: it is obligatory upon him to sacrifice for his son and his son's son who has no father; the fatwa is that it is not obligatory upon him. Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Sharh al-Adahi from al-Za'farani: when the father sacrifices for the minor, then according to the saying of Muhammad and Zufar, he must guarantee, and according to the saying of Imam Abu Yusuf, he does not guarantee, and likewise the guardian. And in al-Yanabi': the mentally deficient and the insane are like the minor; the one who alternates between sanity and insanity is like the sane. And if the insane person is wealthy, his guardian should sacrifice on his behalf from his wealth according to the well-known narrations, and it is narrated that the udhiyah before he sacrifices by it is not obligatory on the wealth of the insane.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the conditions of wealth that make udhiyah obligatory. The text first defines the insolvent (mu'sir) as one whose wealth is insufficient for his needs. If one has waqf property, the relevant factor is the income he receives from it during the days of slaughter—if it reaches 200 dirhams, sacrifice is required; otherwise not. Imam Muhammad reports from Abu Hanifa that if it exceeds 200, it is not obligatory (i.e., nisab is not cumulative across years). For a woman, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider her wealthy if her dowry is payable by a solvent husband, but Abu Hanifa does not consider that as wealth because the dowry may not yet be received. A baker with 200 dirhams worth of wheat must sacrifice, but if he owns a Quran worth a dirham and he can read it, the Quran is considered a necessity (like a tool of worship), so he is not considered wealthy; if he cannot read, it is a commodity and counts toward nisab. Regarding sacrificing for a minor child, al-Hasan reported that Abu Hanifa made it obligatory on the father, but the fatwa is that it is not obligatory. If a father sacrifices for a minor from his own property, Muhammad and Zufar say the father must guarantee (damān) because he acted without permission, while Abu Yusuf says no guarantee, and similarly for a guardian. The mentally deficient (ma'tuh) and insane (majnūn) are like minors—not obligated. A cyclic insane person is treated as sane. The guardian of a wealthy insane person should sacrifice from his money according to the popular narrations, but another narration says it is not obligatory on the insane person's wealth.

Key Terms
موسر (mūsir): One who owns wealth above his basic needs, defined as owning nisab (200 dirhams) after debts and necessities.
معسر (mu‘sir): One whose wealth is insufficient for his basic needs.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers obligation, here 200 dirhams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, is udhiyah obligatory on a baker who has wheat worth 200 dirhams?
A) Yes, because the wheat is his wealth and meets the nisab.
B) No, because wheat is a food staple and thus a necessity.
C) Yes, only if he can also read the Quran.
D) No, because he is a baker and his wealth is from trade.

Key Takeaway
Wealth for udhiyah is measured by nisab of 200 dirhams during the slaughter days, excluding necessary tools and basic needs.

The Woman's Mahr: Does It Make Her Wealthy for Udhiyah?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on counting mahr as wealth for udhiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether a woman's mahr counts toward nisab for udhiyah, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: mahr is not wealth; Sahibayn: it is, if the husband is solvent.

When a woman is owed a dowry (mahr) by her husband, does that debt make her wealthy enough to be obligated for udhiyah? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. This lesson explores the reasoning behind each view and the accepted fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The question is whether a woman's mahr (dowry) is considered wealth (mal) when assessing nisab for udhiyah, if the husband is solvent (mali').
The mahr is a debt owed by the husband, not yet received.
2
State the position of Imam Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the woman is not considered wealthy by the mahr. His reasoning: the mahr is not in her possession; it is a debt that may not be collected easily, or it is akin to future income.
He may also consider it as a 'mal gha'ib' (absent wealth) that does not count for annual obligations.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)
The two disciples consider the woman wealthy by the mahr if the husband is solvent, meaning he is able to pay. They treat it as a current asset because it is a practically recoverable debt.
This is analogous to treating debts owed by a solvent person as wealth for zakat in some views.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
The later Hanafi authorities prefer Abu Hanifa's view. The fatwa is that the woman is not obligated to sacrifice based on the mahr alone.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah supports this tarjih.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, a woman with only an unpaid mahr of 200 dirhams owes no udhiyah. Under the Sahibayn’s view, she must sacrifice if the husband is solvent.
If she actually receives the mahr before the days of slaughter, the difference evaporates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن كتاب الأضحية ماله لا يكفيه فهو معسر. وإن كان العقار وقفاً ينظر؛ إن وجب له في أيام النحر قدر مائتي درهم فعليه الأضحية وإلا فلا. رواه ابن سماعة عن محمد عن الإمام وعنه أنه لا يجب إذا زاد على مائتين. والمرأة تعتبر موسرة بالمهر إذا الزوج ملياً عندهما، وعند الإمام لا تعتبر ملية بذلك. وإن كان خباز عنده حنطة قيمتها مائتا درهم فعليه الأضحية وإن كان عنده مصحف قيمته درهم وهو ممن يحسن القراءة فيه فلا أضحية عليه سواء كان يقرأ فيه أو لا يقرأ فيه، وإن كان لا يحسن أن لا يقرأ فيه فعليه الأضحية. وفي الكافي عن الحسن عن الإمام يجب عليه أن يضحي عن ولده وولد ولده الذي لا أب له؛ والفتوى على أنه لا يجب عليه. وذكر الصدر الشهيد في شرح الأضاحي عن الزعفراني فيما إذا ضحى الأب عن الصغير من فعلى قول محمد وزفر يجب الضمان عليه وعلى قول الإمام أبي يوسف لا يضمن ومثله الوصي. وفي الينابيع والمعتوه والمجنون بمنزلة الصبي والذي يجن ويفيق كالصحيح، ولو كان المجنون موسراً يضحي عنه وليه من ماله في الروايات المشهورة، وروي أن الأضحية قبل يضحي بها لا تجب في مال المجنون.
That the book of Udhiyah: he whose wealth does not suffice him is insolvent. And if the real estate is a waqf, it is considered: if he is entitled to receive during the days of slaughter the amount of two hundred dirhams, then the udhiyah is obligatory upon him, otherwise not. It was narrated by Ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad from the Imam, and from him that it is not obligatory if it exceeds two hundred. And the woman is considered wealthy by the dowry if the husband is solvent according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to the Imam she is not considered wealthy by that. And if a baker has wheat worth two hundred dirhams, then the udhiyah is upon him. And if he has a mushaf worth a dirham while he is one who can read it well, then there is no udhiyah upon him, whether he reads from it or does not. And if he is not skilled in it, then upon him is the udhiyah. And in al-Kafi from al-Hasan from the Imam: it is obligatory upon him to sacrifice for his son and his son's son who has no father; the fatwa is that it is not obligatory upon him. Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Sharh al-Adahi from al-Za'farani: when the father sacrifices for the minor, then according to the saying of Muhammad and Zufar, he must guarantee, and according to the saying of Imam Abu Yusuf, he does not guarantee, and likewise the guardian. And in al-Yanabi': the mentally deficient and the insane are like the minor; the one who alternates between sanity and insanity is like the sane. And if the insane person is wealthy, his guardian should sacrifice on his behalf from his wealth according to the well-known narrations, and it is narrated that the udhiyah before he sacrifices by it is not obligatory on the wealth of the insane.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the conditions of wealth that make udhiyah obligatory. The text first defines the insolvent (mu'sir) as one whose wealth is insufficient for his needs. If one has waqf property, the relevant factor is the income he receives from it during the days of slaughter—if it reaches 200 dirhams, sacrifice is required; otherwise not. Imam Muhammad reports from Abu Hanifa that if it exceeds 200, it is not obligatory (i.e., nisab is not cumulative across years). For a woman, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider her wealthy if her dowry is payable by a solvent husband, but Abu Hanifa does not consider that as wealth because the dowry may not yet be received. A baker with 200 dirhams worth of wheat must sacrifice, but if he owns a Quran worth a dirham and he can read it, the Quran is considered a necessity (like a tool of worship), so he is not considered wealthy; if he cannot read, it is a commodity and counts toward nisab. Regarding sacrificing for a minor child, al-Hasan reported that Abu Hanifa made it obligatory on the father, but the fatwa is that it is not obligatory. If a father sacrifices for a minor from his own property, Muhammad and Zufar say the father must guarantee (damān) because he acted without permission, while Abu Yusuf says no guarantee, and similarly for a guardian. The mentally deficient (ma'tuh) and insane (majnūn) are like minors—not obligated. A cyclic insane person is treated as sane. The guardian of a wealthy insane person should sacrifice from his money according to the popular narrations, but another narration says it is not obligatory on the insane person's wealth.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): Dowry or bridal gift owed by husband to wife.
الصاحبان (al-Ṣāḥibān): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) Hanafi position, does a woman with an unpaid mahr of 200 dirhams from a solvent husband have to perform udhiyah?
A) Yes, because she has a claim to wealth.
B) No, because the mahr is not in her possession.
C) Yes, but only if the husband pays before the days of slaughter.
D) No, because she is a woman.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa is that an unpaid mahr, even from a solvent husband, does not count toward the nisab for udhiyah.

Sacrificing for Minors and the Insane: Obligation and Liability
Rulings on udhiyah for minors and the insane, including liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on udhiyah for minors and the insane, and distinguish the positions on liability (daman) when a father or guardian sacrifices on their behalf.

Core Idea
The fatwa is that a father is not obligated to sacrifice for his minor child, but if he does, he may incur liability.

Children and the insane are not legally responsible for religious obligations, but what about the person who cares for them? This lesson covers the Hanafi rulings on whether a father must sacrifice for his young son, whether the guardian of an insane person should sacrifice from their wealth, and who bears liability if the sacrifice is performed on their behalf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subjects
The passage discusses three categories: the minor child (including grandson with no father), the ma'tuh (mentally deficient), and the majnun (insane).
All are not obligated themselves due to lack of legal capacity.
2
Ruling on sacrificing for the minor
A narration from al-Hasan reports that Abu Hanifa said the father must sacrifice for his minor child. However, the fatwa (relied-upon view) is that it is not obligatory on the father.
The father is not required to use his own wealth; the child is not obligated.
3
Liability (daman) when sacrificing for the minor
If the father or guardian sacrifices for the minor from the minor's own property, there is a dispute: Muhammad and Zufar say he must guarantee (daman) the value because he acted without clear permission. Abu Yusuf says no guarantee.
The guarantee applies if he used the minor's wealth; if he used his own, no issue.
4
Ruling on the insane person
The ma'tuh and majnun are like the minor: they are not obligated. The one who alternates (cyclic insanity) is treated as sane. For a wealthy insane person, the guardian should sacrifice from his wealth according to the well-known narrations, but another narration says it is not obligatory.
The cyclic insane is treated as sane when lucid.

Source Text

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee; liability to compensate for loss or value.
وصي (waṣī): Guardian (executor) appointed to manage an orphan's affairs.
مَعْتُوه (ma‘tūh): Mentally deficient person who is not severely insane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi), is a father obligated to sacrifice for his minor child?
A) Yes, because the father is responsible for the child's religious duties.
B) No, because the child is not personally wealthy.
C) Yes, because the child is part of the household.
D) No, because udhiyah is only for adults.

Key Takeaway
The father is not obligated to sacrifice for his minor child, and if he uses the child's wealth, a dispute on guarantee exists.

intermediate

When Buying a Sheep for Udhiyah Becomes Wajib: Rich vs Poor
Core ruling on rich vs poor with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a rich versus a poor person who buys a sheep with the intention of udhiyah but without verbal designation, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
For the rich, intention alone does not make udhiyah wajib; for the poor, it does – but scholars differ.

This lesson examines the key threshold at which simply buying a sheep with the intention of udhiyah transforms into a binding obligation. The classical texts distinguish sharply between the rich and the poor, with a notable disagreement within the Hanafi school on the poor's case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is buying a sheep with the intention of udhiyah. For a rich person, the ruling is that it does NOT become wajib. For a poor person, some say it becomes wajib by the purchase alone.
The default ruling is that udhiyah is sunnah mu'akkadah for the wealthy, but becomes wajib only through a vow or verbal designation.
2
Locate the Condition
The condition for wajib becoming attached to the sheep is either a vow (nathr) or verbal designation (ījāb). Pure intention (niyya) without speech is insufficient for the rich.
3
Trace the Disagreement on the Poor
Shaykh al-Islam Khoja Zada states in Zahir al-Riwayah that for a poor person, purchase with intention makes it wajib. Al-Za'farani reports from companions that it does not. Al-Sarakhsi and al-Halwani prefer the former as the relied-upon view.
The term 'poor' here refers to someone who is not obligated to offer udhiyah due to financial status.
4
Apply to a Scenario
If a rich man buys a sheep on the 10th of Dhul Hijjah intending udhiyah but never says 'I obligate this for udhiyah', he may sell it or slaughter it voluntarily. If a poor man does the same, according to the mufta bihi, it becomes wajib and he must sacrifice it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

علي أن أضحي بهذه الشاة ولم يذكر بلسانه شيئاً فاشترى شاة بنية الأضحية إن كان المشتري غنياً لا تصير واجبة باتفاق الروايات فله أن يبيعها ويشتري غيرها، وإن كان فقيراً ذكر شيخ الإسلام خواهر زاده في ظاهر الرواية تصير واجبة بنفس الشراء، وروى الزعفراني عن أصحابنا لا تصير واجبة. وأشار إليه شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه وإليه مال شمس الأئمة الحلواني في شرحه وقال : إنه ظاهر الرواية ولو صرح بلسانه والمسألة بحالها تصير واجبة بشراء نية الأضحية إن كان المشتري فقيراً. وفي الخانية : اشترى شاة للأضحية ثم باعها واشترى أخرى في أيام النحر فهذا على وجوه ثلاثة : الأول اشترى شاة ينوي بها الأضحية لا تصير أضحية ما لم يوجبها بلسانه وبه أخذ أبو يوسف وبعض المتأخرين. وفي الكبرى : قال إن فعلت كذا علي أن أضحي لا يكون يميناً رجل اشترى أضحيته وأوجبها فضلت ثم اشترى أخرى فأوجبها ثم وجد الأولى؛ إن كان أوجب الثانية بلسانه فعليه أن يضحي بهما، وإن أوجبها بدلاً عن الأولى فعليه أن يذبح أيهما شاء ولم يفصل بين الفقير والغني، وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند : الفقير إذا أوجب شاة على نفسه هل يحل له أن يأكل منها ؟ قال بديع الدين : نعم . وقال القاضي برهان الدين لا يحل وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند الفقير إذا اشترى شاة للأضحية فسرقت فاشترى مكانها ثم وجد الأولى فعليه أن يضحي بهما، ولو ضلت فليس عليه أن يشتري أخرى مكانها وإن كان غنياً فعليه أن يشتري أخرى مكانها. وفي الواقعات : له مائتا درهم فاشترى بعشرين درهماً أضحية يوم الثلاث وهلكت يوم الأربعاء وجاء يوم النحر إلى آخر أيامه ولا يذبح مصري قبل الصلاة وذبح غيره ويضحي بالجماء والخصي الخميس الأضحى ليس عليه أن يضحي لفقره يوم الأضحى. وفي الفتاوى العتابية : إذا انتقص نصابه يوم الأضحى سقط عنه الزكاة. وعن ابن سلام وكل رجلين أن يشتري كل منهما أضحية فاشتريا يجب عليه أن يضحي بهما. وفي المحيط ولو اشترى شاتين للأضحية فضاعت إحداهما فضحى بالثانية ثم وجدها في أيام النحر فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتعين أحدهما وأيهما ضحى بها فهي المعينة، ولو ضحى الفقير ثم أيسر أعاد وفي رواية وإذا اشترى شاة للأضحية ثم باعها جاز البيع. وفي الأصل : رجل أوجب على نفسه عشر أضاح قالوا لا يلزمه إلا شاتان. قال الصدر الشهيد في واقعاته والظاهر أنه يجب الكل. وفي الظهيرية : والصحيح أنه يجب الكل. وفي الحاوي ولو اشترى شاة ولم يرد أن يضحي بها بل للتجارة يضحي بها ومضى أيام النحر لا يجب عليه أن يتصدق بها. وعن محمد بن سلمة : ثم نوى أن لو ضحى بشاتين لا تكون الأضحية إلا واحدة وفي المحيط الأصح أن تكون الأضحية بهما. وعن الحسن عن أبي حنيفة : لا بأس بالأضحية بالشاة أو بالشاتين. قال الفقيه : وبه نأخذ.. وفي الأصل : الناذر لا يأكل مما نذره ولو أكل فعليه قيمة ما أكل. وفي أضاحي الزعفراني : إن قال الله علي أن أضحي بشاة في أيام النحر ؛ فإن كان موسراً فعليه أن يضحي بشاتين إلا أن يعين بالإيجاب ما يجب عليه، فإن كان فقيراً فعليه شاة. وفي السراجية: إذا قال الله علي أن أضحي بشاة فضحى ببدنة أو ببقرة جاز ا هـ . وفي الشارح : إذا نذروا أراد بها الواجب عليه لا يلزمه غيرها، وإن أراد الواجب بسبب الغنى يلزمه غيرها ا هـ .
And in al-Muntaqa: He bought a sheep to sacrifice with it, then died during the days of sacrifice before sacrificing with it; he may sell it. And whoever is absent from his wealth during the days of sacrifice is considered poor. And it is not hidden that the udhiyah becomes obligatory through a vow. So if he said inwardly 'upon me is to sacrifice with this sheep' and did not utter anything with his tongue, then bought a sheep with the intention of udhiyah: if the buyer is rich, it does not become obligatory by agreement of the narrations, so he may sell it and buy another. And if he is poor, Shaykh al-Islam Khoja Zada mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah that it becomes obligatory by the mere purchase, and al-Za'farani narrated from our companions that it does not become obligatory. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi indicated it in his commentary and Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani inclined to it in his commentary and said it is Zahir al-Riwayah. And if he verbally explicitated and the issue remains, it becomes obligatory by purchasing with the intention of udhiyah if the buyer is poor. And in al-Khaniyya: He bought a sheep for udhiyah, then sold it and bought another during the days of slaughter; this has three aspects: first, he bought a sheep intending udhiyah, it does not become udhiyah as long as he has not obligated it by his tongue, and this is what Abu Yusuf and some later scholars adopted. And in al-Kubra: He said 'if I do such-and-such, upon me is to sacrifice' is not an oath. A man bought his udhiyah and obligated it, then it got lost, then he bought another and obligated it, then found the first: if he obligated the second by his tongue, he must sacrifice both; if he obligated it as an alternative for the first, he may slaughter whichever he wishes, and he did not differentiate between the poor and the rich. And in the Fatawa of the people of Samarkand: if a poor person has obligated a sheep upon himself, may he eat from it? Badī' al-Din said yes, and Qadi Burhan al-Din said no. And in the Fatawa of the people of Samarkand: if a poor person bought a sheep for udhiyah and it was stolen, so he bought another in its place, then found the first, he must sacrifice both. And if it was lost, he is not obliged to buy another in its place; but if he is rich, he must buy another in its place. And in al-Waqi'at: He has two hundred dirhams, so he bought with twenty dirhams an udhiyah on Tuesday, and it perished on Wednesday, and the day of slaughter came until its last days... (the text continues with various rulings until the beginning of the next section).
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles numerous rulings from different sources regarding the process of buying a sheep for udhiyah, the effect of intention versus verbal obligation, and cases of vows (nathr). The key principle is that simply buying a sheep with the intention of udhiyah does not make it a wajib udhiyah unless one verbally designates it (ījāb bil-lisān). There is disagreement on whether a poor person's purchase with intention makes it wajib; the majority view (Zahir al-Riwayah) according to Khoja Zada and al-Sarakhsi is that it becomes wajib by the purchase alone, while al-Za'farani reports it does not. For the rich, it is not wajib by agreement. Other rulings address scenarios like lost sheep, multiple sheep, sale, and eating from a vowed udhiyah for a poor person, with differences among scholars. The passage also includes that a person who vows to sacrifice ten animals is only required to sacrifice two according to some, but the correct view (as per al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Zahiriyya) is that he must sacrifice all. It ends with a note from al-Za'farani's treatise on udhiyah detailing the difference between a rich and poor person's vow.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, here specifically sacrificing a sheep
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): Verbal designation of a specific animal for udhiyah
الغني (al-ghanī): Wealthy person who possesses nisab for udhiyah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling for a rich person who buys a sheep with the intention of udhiyah but does not verbally designate it?
A) It becomes wajib and must be sacrificed.
B) It does not become wajib; he may sell it.
C) It becomes wajib only if it is a male sheep.
D) It is makruh to sell it.

Key Takeaway
A rich person's purchase of a sheep for udhiyah does not make it wajib; for a poor person, it does, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Verbal Designation (ījāb) and the Intra-Hanafi Debate
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on verbal designation requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why many Hanafi scholars require verbal designation (ījāb) for a sheep to become wajib udhiyah, and identify the position of Abu Yusuf and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Intention alone is insufficient; verbal obligation (ījāb) is required, except for the poor according to the prevalent view.

This lesson dives deeper into the requirement of speaking the obligation. While the previous lesson focused on wealth status, here we see that some scholars—especially Abu Yusuf—insist on verbal designation even for the poor. This intra-Hanafi debate shapes fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the General Principle
Udhiyah does not become wajib on a specific animal until the owner verbally says 'I obligate this for udhiyah' (awjabtu). The text calls this 'ījāb bil-lisān'.
2
Identify the Disagreement
Abu Yusuf and some later scholars hold that verbal designation is always required, regardless of wealth. This is stated in al-Khaniyya: 'it does not become udhiyah as long as he has not obligated it by his tongue'.
Al-Khaniyya attributes this view to Abu Yusuf and 'some later scholars'.
3
Note the Exception for the Poor
The previous lesson showed that for the poor, some scholars treat intention as sufficient. But Abu Yusuf does not make that exception. The text in al-Khaniyya does not differentiate between rich and poor.
This conflict is part of the broader ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Apply to a Scenario
If a poor man buys a sheep with intention but no words, Abu Yusuf says it is not wajib. The mufta bihi (according to al-Halwani etc.) says it is wajib. Which should a mufti follow today? The answer is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى اشترى شاة ليضحي بها فمات في أيام الأضحية قبل أن يضحي بها فله أن يبيعها، ومن كان غائباً عن ماله في أيام الأضحية فهو فقير، ولا يخفى أن الأضحية تصير واجبة بالنذر فلو قال كلاماً نفسياً الله علي أن أضحي بهذه الشاة ولم يذكر بلسانه شيئاً فاشترى شاة بنية الأضحية إن كان المشتري غنياً لا تصير واجبة باتفاق الروايات فله أن يبيعها ويشتري غيرها، وإن كان فقيراً ذكر شيخ الإسلام خواهر زاده في ظاهر الرواية تصير واجبة بنفس الشراء، وروى الزعفراني عن أصحابنا لا تصير واجبة. وأشار إليه شمس الأئمة السرخسي في شرحه وإليه مال شمس الأئمة الحلواني في شرحه وقال : إنه ظاهر الرواية ولو صرح بلسانه والمسألة بحالها تصير واجبة بشراء نية الأضحية إن كان المشتري فقيراً. وفي الخانية : اشترى شاة للأضحية ثم باعها واشترى أخرى في أيام النحر فهذا على وجوه ثلاثة : الأول اشترى شاة ينوي بها الأضحية لا تصير أضحية ما لم يوجبها بلسانه وبه أخذ أبو يوسف وبعض المتأخرين. وفي الكبرى : قال إن فعلت كذا علي أن أضحي لا يكون يميناً رجل اشترى أضحيته وأوجبها فضلت ثم اشترى أخرى فأوجبها ثم وجد الأولى؛ إن كان أوجب الثانية بلسانه فعليه أن يضحي بهما، وإن أوجبها بدلاً عن الأولى فعليه أن يذبح أيهما شاء ولم يفصل بين الفقير والغني، وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند : الفقير إذا أوجب شاة على نفسه هل يحل له أن يأكل منها ؟ قال بديع الدين : نعم . وقال القاضي برهان الدين لا يحل وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند الفقير إذا اشترى شاة للأضحية فسرقت فاشترى مكانها ثم وجد الأولى فعليه أن يضحي بهما، ولو ضلت فليس عليه أن يشتري أخرى مكانها وإن كان غنياً فعليه أن يشتري أخرى مكانها. وفي الواقعات : له مائتا درهم فاشترى بعشرين درهماً أضحية يوم الثلاث وهلكت يوم الأربعاء وجاء يوم النحر إلى آخر أيامه ولا يذبح مصري قبل الصلاة وذبح غيره ويضحي بالجماء والخصي الخميس الأضحى ليس عليه أن يضحي لفقره يوم الأضحى. وفي الفتاوى العتابية : إذا انتقص نصابه يوم الأضحى سقط عنه الزكاة. وعن ابن سلام وكل رجلين أن يشتري كل منهما أضحية فاشتريا يجب عليه أن يضحي بهما. وفي المحيط ولو اشترى شاتين للأضحية فضاعت إحداهما فضحى بالثانية ثم وجدها في أيام النحر فلا شيء عليه لأنه لم يتعين أحدهما وأيهما ضحى بها فهي المعينة، ولو ضحى الفقير ثم أيسر أعاد وفي رواية وإذا اشترى شاة للأضحية ثم باعها جاز البيع. وفي الأصل : رجل أوجب على نفسه عشر أضاح قالوا لا يلزمه إلا شاتان. قال الصدر الشهيد في واقعاته والظاهر أنه يجب الكل. وفي الظهيرية : والصحيح أنه يجب الكل. وفي الحاوي ولو اشترى شاة ولم يرد أن يضحي بها بل للتجارة يضحي بها ومضى أيام النحر لا يجب عليه أن يتصدق بها. وعن محمد بن سلمة : ثم نوى أن لو ضحى بشاتين لا تكون الأضحية إلا واحدة وفي المحيط الأصح أن تكون الأضحية بهما. وعن الحسن عن أبي حنيفة : لا بأس بالأضحية بالشاة أو بالشاتين. قال الفقيه : وبه نأخذ.. وفي الأصل : الناذر لا يأكل مما نذره ولو أكل فعليه قيمة ما أكل. وفي أضاحي الزعفراني : إن قال الله علي أن أضحي بشاة في أيام النحر ؛ فإن كان موسراً فعليه أن يضحي بشاتين إلا أن يعين بالإيجاب ما يجب عليه، فإن كان فقيراً فعليه شاة. وفي السراجية: إذا قال الله علي أن أضحي بشاة فضحى ببدنة أو ببقرة جاز ا هـ . وفي الشارح : إذا نذروا أراد بها الواجب عليه لا يلزمه غيرها، وإن أراد الواجب بسبب الغنى يلزمه غيرها ا هـ .
And in al-Muntaqa: He bought a sheep to sacrifice with it, then died during the days of sacrifice before sacrificing with it; he may sell it. And whoever is absent from his wealth during the days of sacrifice is considered poor. And it is not hidden that the udhiyah becomes obligatory through a vow. So if he said inwardly 'upon me is to sacrifice with this sheep' and did not utter anything with his tongue, then bought a sheep with the intention of udhiyah: if the buyer is rich, it does not become obligatory by agreement of the narrations, so he may sell it and buy another. And if he is poor, Shaykh al-Islam Khoja Zada mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah that it becomes obligatory by the mere purchase, and al-Za'farani narrated from our companions that it does not become obligatory. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi indicated it in his commentary and Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani inclined to it in his commentary and said it is Zahir al-Riwayah. And if he verbally explicitated and the issue remains, it becomes obligatory by purchasing with the intention of udhiyah if the buyer is poor. And in al-Khaniyya: He bought a sheep for udhiyah, then sold it and bought another during the days of slaughter; this has three aspects: first, he bought a sheep intending udhiyah, it does not become udhiyah as long as he has not obligated it by his tongue, and this is what Abu Yusuf and some later scholars adopted. And in al-Kubra: He said 'if I do such-and-such, upon me is to sacrifice' is not an oath. A man bought his udhiyah and obligated it, then it got lost, then he bought another and obligated it, then found the first: if he obligated the second by his tongue, he must sacrifice both; if he obligated it as an alternative for the first, he may slaughter whichever he wishes, and he did not differentiate between the poor and the rich. And in the Fatawa of the people of Samarkand: if a poor person has obligated a sheep upon himself, may he eat from it? Badī' al-Din said yes, and Qadi Burhan al-Din said no. And in the Fatawa of the people of Samarkand: if a poor person bought a sheep for udhiyah and it was stolen, so he bought another in its place, then found the first, he must sacrifice both. And if it was lost, he is not obliged to buy another in its place; but if he is rich, he must buy another in its place. And in al-Waqi'at: He has two hundred dirhams, so he bought with twenty dirhams an udhiyah on Tuesday, and it perished on Wednesday, and the day of slaughter came until its last days... (the text continues with various rulings until the beginning of the next section).

الإيجاب باللسان (al-ījāb bil-lisān): Verbal designation of the sacrificial animal

Vows (Nathr) for Udhiyah: Multiple Animals and Eating from the Sacrifice
Rulings on nathr for udhiyah: multiple animals and consumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for vows (nathr) regarding udhiyah, including the number of animals required and the permissibility of eating from a vowed sacrifice.

Core Idea
A vow to sacrifice multiple animals obligates all; a poor person who vows may or may not eat from it, depending on the scholar.

This lesson covers the latter part of the passage concerning vows. Vows (nathr) make udhiyah wajib even for someone who was not originally obligated. The text discusses cases where one vows ten animals, and whether one may eat from a vowed sacrifice if poor.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Effect of a Vow
A vow to sacrifice an animal makes that sacrifice wajib. This is explicit: 'ولا يخفى أن الأضحية تصير واجبة بالنذر'.
2
Interpret the Vow of Multiple Animals
If a person says 'I vow to sacrifice ten animals', there is disagreement. Some say only two are required (the minimum for udhiyah? Actually a person can sacrifice one sheep per person, but multiple are optional). The text says: 'قالوا لا يلزمه إلا شاتان' (some say only two are required). However, al-Sadr al-Shahid and the correct view say all ten are required.
This shows the principle that vows are interpreted strictly.
3
Address Eating from a Vowed Sacrifice for a Poor Person
If a poor person vows a sheep, can he eat from it? The text reports a difference: Badī' al-Din says yes, Qadi Burhan al-Din says no. The mufta bihi may be that he cannot, as a vowed animal becomes like a sacrifice that must be given away.
This relates to the general rule that one cannot eat from a vowed animal.
4
Apply to a Scenario
A poor man vows to sacrifice three sheep. According to the correct view, he must sacrifice all three. He may not eat from them. If he does, he must pay the value.

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles numerous rulings from different sources regarding the process of buying a sheep for udhiyah, the effect of intention versus verbal obligation, and cases of vows (nathr). The key principle is that simply buying a sheep with the intention of udhiyah does not make it a wajib udhiyah unless one verbally designates it (ījāb bil-lisān). There is disagreement on whether a poor person's purchase with intention makes it wajib; the majority view (Zahir al-Riwayah) according to Khoja Zada and al-Sarakhsi is that it becomes wajib by the purchase alone, while al-Za'farani reports it does not. For the rich, it is not wajib by agreement. Other rulings address scenarios like lost sheep, multiple sheep, sale, and eating from a vowed udhiyah for a poor person, with differences among scholars. The passage also includes that a person who vows to sacrifice ten animals is only required to sacrifice two according to some, but the correct view (as per al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Zahiriyya) is that he must sacrifice all. It ends with a note from al-Za'farani's treatise on udhiyah detailing the difference between a rich and poor person's vow.

Key Terms
الناذر لا يأكل مما نذره (al-nādhir lā ya'kulu mimmā nadharahu): The one who vows does not eat from what he vowed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, what is the ruling on a person who vows to sacrifice ten sheep?
A) He must sacrifice only two.
B) He must sacrifice all ten.
C) He must sacrifice one for each year.
D) He may choose any number.

Key Takeaway
A vow to sacrifice multiple animals obligates all of them; a poor person who vows may not eat from the sacrifice according to the stronger view.
